MinMax Recurrent Neural Cascades
Alessandro Ronca
rnc.lsn@gmail.com
Abstract
We show that the MinMax algebra provides a form of recurrence that is expressively
powerful, efficiently implementable, and most importantly it is not affected by
vanishing or exploding gradient. We call MinMax Recurrent Neural Cascades
(RNCs) the models obtained by cascading several layers of neurons that employ
such recurrence. We show that MinMax RNCs enjoy many favourable theoretical
properties. First, their formal expressivity includes all regular languages, arguably
the maximal expressivity for a finite-memory system. Second, they can be evaluated
in parallel with a runtime that is logarithmic in the input length given enough
processors-and they can also be evaluated sequentially. Third, their state and
activations are bounded uniformly for all input lengths. Fourth, at almost all points,
their loss gradient exists and it is bounded. Fifth, they do not exhibit a vanishing
state gradient: the gradient of a state w.r.t. a past state can have constant value one
regardless of the time distance between the two states. Finally, we find empirical
evidence that the favourable theoretical properties of MinMax RNCs are matched
by their practical capabilities: they are able to perfectly solve a number of synthetic
tasks, showing superior performance compared to the considered state-of-the-art
recurrent neural networks; also, we train a MinMax RNC of 127M parameters on
next-token prediction, and the obtained model shows competitive performance for
its size, providing evidence of the potential of MinMax RNCs on real-world tasks.
Introduction
Recurrence is a natural mechanism to design models capable of processing sequences, as it allows a
model to maintain a state and hence process one sequence element at a time while having the required
information about the previous elements available in its the state. However, it poses significant
challenges in controlling state and gradient norms for increasing sequence length, namely keeping
them from exploding or vanishing [1]. Early RNNs [2] are based on non-linear recurrence: their
state is bounded but has a vanishing gradient, they are expressively powerful as they capture all
regular languages [3], they can be evaluated sequentially in time linear in sequence length, but
they are not parallelisable. LSTM [4] offers a mitigating solution leading to practical applicability.
Transformers [5] adopted a radically different solution by processing sequences using attention in
place of recurrence, they are highly parallelisable, and they have been greatly successful in real-world
applications. Their main drawback is quadratic complexity in sequence length, and they have also
been shown to have a formal expressivity limited to a subclass of the regular languages [6, 7]. This
has motivated research into new recurrent architectures. Aside from sLSTM [8] that further refines
the LSTM solution, the main focus has been on linear recurrence, possibly with gating, leading to a
great variety of architectures [9-30]. They have re-established recurrence as a relevant mechanism,
as they are competitive with Transformers. An immediate advantage of linear recurrence is that it can
be efficiently evaluated via parallel scan [31], and the challenge of unbounded state and gradient
norms is solved through advanced parametrisations of the state-update matrix. Fundamentally the
parametrisations ensure a state-update matrix At with norm strictly less than one, as otherwise any
linear recurrence xt = Atxt−1 + bt diverges for bt > 0. At the same time, this makes the recurrence
contracting, with two important implications: (i) the state gradient is necessarily vanishing; (ii) for
Preprint.
arXiv:2605.06384v1 [cs.LG] 7 May 2026

any sequence bt that is constant or just converging, the recurrence will eventually collapse the entire
state space to a single point x⋆. Theoretically, several expressivity results have been shown, with
expressivities ranging from the star-free to all regular languages [17, 25, 32]. However, [33] observes
a discrepancy between the proven star-free expressivity of Mamba [14] and its inability to learn an
elementary star-free language when empirically evaluated. They explain the discrepancy by showing
that the theoretical expressivity may be given in part by brittle instantiations of the model, that achieve
their capabilities by separating states with a margin converging to zero (for states converging to
a common x⋆). Thus, they define η-finiteness as a characterisation that excludes such behaviour,
showing that the η-finiteness requirement makes the linear model Mamba [14] lose its ability to
recognise all star-free languages, whereas it has no impact on the non-linear model sLSTM [8]. For
the above reasons, expressivity results under the η-finiteness requirement may be considered more
reliable, as any additional expressivity is given by model instantances that rely on separating points
with a vanishing margin, which may not be feasible in practice.
Contribution.
We show that the MinMax recurrence xt = (At ⊗xt−1) ⊕bt-obtained from
standard linear recurrence by replacing addition with max and multiplication with min-allows for
building recurrent networks that enjoy the best theoretical properties of the models discussed above,
matched by their practical capabilities in our empirical evaluation. Specifically, we show the results
below for the class of MinMax Recurrent Neural Cascades (MinMax RNCs), obtained by cascading
layers of neurons that employ MinMax recurrence with At and bt given by feedforward networks.
• Expressivity. We show that η-finite MinMax Neurons can realise all fundamental semiautomata
(Theorems 2, 3), making MinMax RNCs capable of recognising all regular languages (Theorem 1).
Furthermore, we show that the state dimension of a neuron corresponds to the permutation degree
of the semiautomata it can realise. Thus, scalar states suffice for all star-free languages; and more
generally, n-dimensional states suffice for all languages whose canonical automaton admits a
cascade decomposition into semiautomata of permutation degree at most n-considering that n
can be factorially smaller than the number of states of each semiautomaton, which can in turn be
exponentially smaller than the number of states of the canonical automaton.
• Complexity. MinMax recurrence of state dimension N can be evaluated sequentially in O(TN 2) for
T the sequence length (Theorems 4, 5). It can also be evaluated via parallel scan, since associativity
and distributivity of {⊗, ⊕} yield an associative operator ⊕⊗to compose recurrence steps; as a result,
MinMax recurrence can be evaluated with parallel depth O(log T log N) and work O(TN 3), hence
in time O(log T log N) given enough processors (Theorems 4, 6).
• Stability and gradient. MinMax RNCs have bounded states and outputs (Theorem 7), bounded
gradient (Theorem 8), and non-vanishing gradient (Theorem 9, Corollary 3). One way to see it
is that MinMax recurrence is a piece-wise linear operator with constant unit norm, hence neither
contracting nor expanding. This ensures the above properties, and also helps understanding why it
achieves full expressivity under the η-finiteness requirement.
• Practical performance. For the empirical evaluation we focus on MinMax RNCs of state degree one.
We observe they can perfectly solve the considered star-free tasks, generalising up to length 1M,
and outperforming the considered SOTA models-Mamba [14], mLSTM [8], xLSTM-L [34] based
on linear recurrence, and sLSTM [8] based on non-linear recurrence (Section 7). Furthermore, we
train a MinMax RNC of 127M parameters for next-token prediction on OpenWebText, reaching a
loss comparable to GPT-2 (Section 7), and hence gaining evidence that MinMax RNCs still perform
well when their size is scaled up, and also of their potential in real-world applications.
The paper includes proof sketches of all results, with full proofs and additional details in the appendix.
Preliminaries
For i, j ∈N with i ≤j, we define [i..j] := {i, i + 1, . . . , j} and [j] := [1..j]. For X a set, X+ is the
set of all non-empty sequences of elements of X, and X∗extends X+ with the empty sequence.
MinMax algebra.
For a, b ∈R, we define the operators a⊕b := max(a, b) and a⊙b := min(a, b);
they apply component-wise to vectors and matrices; they are associative, commutative, and ⊙
distributes over ⊕. For A ∈Rm×p, B ∈Rp×n, the operator A ⊗B yields C ∈Rm×n with
Ci,j = Lp
k=1 Ai,k ⊙Bk,j; it is associative and distributes over ⊕; B ∈Rn is seen as B ∈Rn×1.
Dynamical systems.
A (dynamical) system is a tuple S = ⟨X, U, f, x0, Y, h⟩where X, U, Y are
metric spaces, f : X × U →X and h : X × U × X →Y are continuous functions, and x0 ∈X,
calling X the state space, U the input space, f the dynamics function, x0 the initial state, Y the
output space, h the output function, and D = ⟨X, U, f⟩the dynamics. For every input sequence
u = u1, . . . , ut ∈U +, dynamics D inductively determine the states xt = f(xt−1, ut) starting from
any given x0 ∈X, and hence they define the map D(x0, u) = xt; then, system S produces the
outputs yt = h(xt−1, ut, xt), and hence it defines the map S(u) = yt.
Compositions.
Given systems S1 = ⟨X1, U, f1, x0
1, Z, h1⟩and S2 = ⟨X2, Z, f2, x0
2, Y, h2⟩, their
cascade composition S1 ⋉S2 is the system S = ⟨X, U, f, x0, Y, h⟩having state space X = X1 ×X2,
initial state x0 = ⟨x0
1, x0
2⟩, dynamics function f(⟨x1, x2⟩, u) = ⟨x′
1, f2(x2, h1(x1, u, x′
1))⟩for
x′
1 = f1(x1, u1), and output function h(⟨x1, x2⟩, u, ⟨x′
1, x′
2⟩) = h2(x2, h1(x1, u, x′
1), x′
2). Similarly,
dynamics can be cascaded as D1 ⋉D2 by seeing D1 as having an identity input function, and they
can be composed in parallel as D1 ∥D2 by seeing D1 as having a void output function.
Multi-Layer Perceptron.
A Multi-Layer Perceptron (MLP) is aff1◦ϕ◦· · ·◦affn−1◦ϕ◦affn where
each affi is an affine operator in standard real-valued arithmetic, and ϕ : R →R is a non-polynomial
continuously-differentiable function applied element-wise. For every ϵ > 0 and every continuous
function f : X ⊆Rm →Rn with X compact, there is an MLP that ϵ-approximates f [35]. A
normalised MLP is an MLP prepended with a normalisation function such as LayerNorm [36], and a
normalised MLP with residual connections is f(x) = proj(x) + g(x) for g a normalised MLP.
Algebraic Automata Theory (AAT).
An automaton is a system A = ⟨Q, Σ, δ, q0, Γ, θ⟩where
Q, Σ, Γ are finite spaces under the discrete metric, with Σ and Γ called alphabets and ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩called
a semiautomaton. For σ ∈Σ, the state transformation δσ of D is δσ(q) = δ(q, σ). Semiautomaton D
is an identity-reset semiautomaton if each transformation δσ is the identity function or a constant func-
tion, and it is a permutation semiautomaton if each transformation δσ is a bijection. A semiautomaton
D realises a semiautomaton D′ when there is a way to assign the states and inputs of D′ to the ones
of D in a consistent way; the realisability relation is transitive and closed under cascading. By the
Krohn-Rhodes theorem, every semiautomaton D can be realised by a cascade D1 ⋉· · · ⋉Dn where
each Di is an identity-reset or permutation semiautomaton closely connected to D. A semiautomaton
is group-free if realised by a cascade of identity-reset semiautomata. A function F : Σ+ →Γ over
alphabets is (group-free) regular if it is defined by some (group-free) automaton. A language is a
subset of Σ+, it is (star-free) regular if its indicator function is (group-free) regular. Cf. [37, 38].
Metric Automata Theory (MAT) [33].
To relate a system S to a function F : Σ+ →Γ over
alphabets, one says that S can implement F if there are a continuous encoder enc and decoder dec
such that F(w) = dec(S(enc(w))) with enc applied element-wise. Automata theory can be used to
establish whether S can implement F whenever S is η-finite. System S is η-finite if so are its input
and state space. For our purposes, a space X ⊆R is η-finite if it is the union of a finite number of
closed disjoint intervals, called η-components, and every space X1 × · · · × Xn is η-finite if so is each
Xi ⊆R. Function F can be implemented by an η-finite system S iff F can be implemented by an
automaton whose semiautomaton is realised by the η-canonical semiautomaton of the dynamics of S,
obtained from the dynamics of S by seeing each input and state η-component as a single element.
Model
We define MinMax Recurrent Neurons as the neural version of MinMax Recurrent Units, which
formalise arbitrary dynamics based on MinMax recurrence-see also Examples 1, 2.
Definition 1. We define a MinMax Recurrent Unit with input functions R : U ⊆Rm →Rn×n and
s : U ⊆Rm →Rn as dynamics D = ⟨X, U, f⟩having state space X ⊆Rn and dynamics function:
f(x, u) =
 R(u) ⊗x

⊕s(u).
We call R the reset function and s the set function, and we also write D = ⟨X, U, f | R, s⟩. Then,
dynamics D are a MinMax Recurrent Neuron if R and s are MLPs possibly normalised.
■
MinMax RNCs are obtained by cascading layers of multiple MinMax Recurrent Neurons where they
are arranged in parallel. The definition is in terms of composition operators (§2). The diagram of a
two-unit layer is shown in Figure 1, and cascading corresponds to stacking such diagrams.

Definition 2. A MinMax Recurrent Cascade is a cascade composition S1 ⋉· · ·⋉Sn where each Si is
a MinMax Recurrent Layer, defined as a system having dynamics Di given by a parallel composition
Di,1 ∥· · · ∥Di,ni of MinMax Recurrent Units. A MinMax Recurrent Neural Cascade (MinMax
RNC) is a MinMax Recurrent Cascade where (i) each Unit Di,j is a MinMax Recurrent Neuron,
and (ii) the output function of each Layer Si is an MLP possibly normalised and with residual
connnections. The state degree of S is the maximum state dimension of any Di,j.
■
We present two examples to provide intuition on the mechanics of MinMax Neurons.
Example 1. For the MinMax Neuron D = ⟨R, R2, f | R, s⟩having reset function R(a, b) = a and
set function s(a, b) = b, for an initial state x0 = 0 and input sequence u = (0, 2), (7, 0), (5, 0), (0, 1),
if we write ut,1, ut,2 for the first and second input component at time t, the state sequence is:
x1 = (u1,1 ⊗x0) ⊕u1,2 = (0 ⊙0) ⊕2 = 2,
x2 = (u2,1 ⊗x1) ⊕u2,2 = (7 ⊙2) ⊕0 = 2,
x3 = (u3,1 ⊗x2) ⊕u3,2 = (5 ⊙2) ⊕0 = 2,
x4 = (u4,1 ⊗x3) ⊕u4,2 = (0 ⊙2) ⊕1 = 1.
At step t, the neuron stores s(ut) if R(ut) ≤s(ut), and keeps xt−1 if s(ut) ≤xt−1 ≤R(ut).

■
Expressivity
We characterise the expressivity of MinMax RNCs in terms of the functions over finite alphabets they
can implement, using AAT and MAT as presented in §2 and further in the appendix. Next we provide
a group-theoretic notion of degree, that we show to characterise the state degree of MinMax RNCs.
Definition 3. The (permutation) degree of a group G is the minimum number n such that G is
isomorphic to a subgroup of the symmetric group Sn. The degree of a permutation semiautomaton is
the degree of its transition group. The degree of a regular function F is the maximum between one
and the degree of any simple group that is a factor of a subgroup of the transition monoid of F.
We state the main theorem regarding the expressivity of MinMax RNCs.
Theorem 1. Every regular function can be implemented by a MinMax RNC of state degree equal to
the degree of the function. Any function that is not regular cannot be implemented by a MinMax RNC.
Any function that is not group-free cannot be implemented by a MinMax RNC of state degree one.
Proof sketch. For the first result, implementing a regular function amounts to realising the semiau-
tomaton A of an automaton that implements it. Then A can be realised by a cascade of identity-reset
and permutation semiautomata of degree bounded by the degree of the function; in particular, they
are all identity-reset in the group-free case. The key step is to show η-finite MinMax Neurons that
can realise such semiautomata, as we do in the next section (Theorems 2 and 3). Such Neurons can
be composed into a cascade that can realise A and hence implement the given regular function. The
second result follows from the fact that, for every MinMax RNC implementing a function over a finite
alphabet, there is an equivalent one having a finite state space, implying that the function is regular.
For the third result, we observe that, for any fixed scalar values r, s and state x0, the recurrence
xt = (r ⊗xt−1) ⊕s yields a state sequence that converges to a constant in two steps, meaning that
MinMax Units of state degree one cannot realise permutation semiautomata, which prevents them,
and cascades thereof, from implementing any function that is not group-free.
Thus, we have obtain the exact expressitivity of MinMax RNCs and their subclass of state degree one.
Corollary 1. The functions that can be implemented by MinMax RNCs are the regular functions, and
the group-free functions if restricted to state degree one. The languages that can be recognised by
MinMax RNCs are the regular languages, and the star-free languages if restricted to state degree one.
4.1
Capabilities of MinMax Recurrent Neurons to Realise Fundamental Semiautomata
We show the core abilities underlying the expressivity of MinMax RNCs. First we present Theorem 2,
focusing on realising identity-reset semiautomata-Example 1 corresponds to this case. Intuitively, it
requires storing information and keeping it unchanged as long as needed. More formally, it requires
dynamics to have a state transformation f(·, u) acting as an identity, and a set of state transformations
{f(·, ui)}i acting as constant functions. For MinMax Neurons, when s ≤r the transformation
fr,s(x) = (r ⊙x) ⊕s is identity for all x ∈[s, r], and it is constant fr,s(x) = s when s > r.
Theorem 2. Every identity-reset semiautomaton can be realised by an η-finite MinMax Recurrent
Neuron having state dimension one.
Proof sketch. Let A = ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩be an identity-reset semiautomaton having states Q = {q1, . . . , qn}
and inputs Σ = {σ1, . . . , σm}. We first define a MinMax Recurrent Unit D that realises it, and then
we turn it into a Neuron. We define D = ⟨X, U, f | R, s⟩as having states X = Sn

Next we present Theorem 3, that focuses on realising any permutation semiautomaton A-Example 2
corresponds to this case. It requires dynamics to admit a set of state transformations {f(·, ui)}i acting
as bijections. This could be shown directly using a state dimension equal to the number of states
of A-we show it in the appendix. Here we adopt an intermediate characterisation of permutation
semiautomata, to show a stronger result connecting the state dimension of a MinMax Neuron to the
degree n of A-always bounded by the number of states, which can be as large as n!. Semiautomaton
A can be realised by a semiautomaton AS where every state is an arrangement i = ⟨i1, . . . , in⟩of
the indices [1..n], and each transformation δσ(i) rearranges i into ⟨iπ(1), . . . , iπ(n)⟩for π a bijection
determined by σ. Thus, we can realise AS in place of A, and we can do so by representing directly
its n-dimensional states using vectors of Rn. In a MinMax recurrence, the multiplication x′ = R ⊗x
allows for assigning any position of x to any position of x′. For instance, in x′

we can obtain x′

Complexity
We analyse the sequential and parallel complexity of evaluating MinMax RNCs. First we analyse
the complexity of evaluating MinMax recurrence for T steps, which amounts to computing all states
xt = (Rt ⊗xt−1) ⊕st given x0 ∈Rdstate, Rt ∈Rdstate×dstate, and st ∈Rdstate for t ∈[1..T].
Theorem 4. MinMax recurrence can be evaluated sequentially in time O(T · d2
state); and also in
parallel with depth O(log T · log dstate) and work O(T · d3
state).
Proof sketch. The straightforward sequential algorithm that computes the states by evaluating xt =
(Rt ⊗xt−1) ⊕st for t from 1 to T runs in the specified time since the most expensive operation at
each step is the matrix-vector multiplication Rt ⊗xt−1 which can be done in O(d2
state). To obtain a
parallel algorithm, we observe that letting τt(x) := (Rt ⊗x) ⊕st, we have xt = (τ1 ◦· · · ◦τt)(x0),
and each composition τ1 ◦· · · ◦τt can be computed via parallel scan, cf. [39-41]. Specifically,
parallel scan evaluates all prefixes of an expression over any given monoid-i.e., expressions where
several elements are combined via an associative operator, for which some element acts as identity.
In our case, the relevant monoid is given by the set of pairs ⟨Rt, st⟩together with the operator
⟨R, s⟩⊕⊗⟨R′, s⟩:= ⟨R′ ⊗R, (R′ ⊗s)⊕s′⟩, and an identity element ⟨E, e⟩is built from the minimum
and maximum scalars appearing in any Rt, st. Then we have (τ1 ◦· · · τt)(x) = (R1:t ⊗x) ⊕s1:t
with ⟨R1:t, s1:t⟩= ⟨R1, s1⟩⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗⟨Rt, st⟩. Thus, all ⟨R1:t, s1:t⟩can be computed via parallel scan,
and hence all states xt. The resulting parallel algorithm has depth and work as claimed, noting that
some extra work is carried out compared to the sequential case due to matrix multiplication in ⊕⊗.
Having the above, developing an algorithm for MinMax RNCs and analysing its complexity is
straightforward. Still, an overall bound may provide useful insights, and hence we next analyse the
complexity of evaluating MinMax RNCs. The algorithmic task consists in computing the sequence
of outputs of a MinMax RNC S over an input u, where an algorithm is given a specification of S in
terms of its parameters, along with the input sequence u. In the following bounds, we write T for
the length of the input sequence, nlayers for the number of layers, dstate for the state degree, and d
for the maximum dimension of the input space, the output space, the hidden layers of its MLPs, and
the state space of any layer. In order to provide more intelligible bounds, here we assume that the
number of hidden layers of MLPs is fixed (and the general case is given in the appendix).
Theorem 5. MinMax RNCs can be evaluated sequentially in time O
 T · nlayers · d · (d + d2
state)

.
Theorem 6. MinMax RNCs can be evaluated in parallel with work and depth bounded as follows:
work: O
 T · nlayers · d · (d + d2
state)

,
depth: O
 nlayers · (log T · log dstate + log d)

.
To provide an overview on the impact of the sequence length T on the runtime, we state the following
bound for RNCs of any fixed size, which is a direct consequence of the above theorem noting that
work and depth translate to runtime by Brent’s Scheduling Principle, cf. [31].
Corollary 2. MinMax RNCs of fixed size can be evaluated in time O(log T) over Θ(T) processors.
Stability and Gradient
We derive bounds on the states and outputs of parametric MinMax RNCs, as well as on their gradients.
A parametric MinMax RNC is one where the scalars of its MLPs are parameters-explicit def. in §B.
First we show that states and outputs are uniformly bounded (for any input length), as long as the
parameter and input values come from a bounded set, in line with BIBS and BIBO stability. It is an
important property in itself, and we need it below to show that gradients are bounded.
Theorem 7 (Stability). Let S be a parametric MinMax RNC having a bounded input space U and a
bounded parameter space Θ, and let Sθ be S with parameters instantiated to θ ∈Θ. There exists a
constant B ∈R such that, for every θ ∈Θ and every u1 · · · ut ∈U +, we have max{∥xt∥, ∥yt∥} ≤
B where xt and yt are the state and output of Sθ at time t on input u1 · · · ut.
Proof sketch. MinMax recursion only propagates values computed by the MLPs, which are contin-
uous and hence map bounded sets to bounded sets; but then U, Θ are bounded by assumption and
hence values from previous layers and time steps are bounded by induction.
We analyse the loss gradient of MinMax RNCs w.r.t. parameters, noting that is not guaranteed to
exist everywhere due to the min and max operations. However, we show it exists at almost all points
of the parameter space, and at such points it is bounded in a uniform manner (for any input length).
Theorem 8 (Bounded gradient). Let S be a parametric MinMax RNC with U, Y, Θ its input, output,
and parameter space, respectively. Let loss : Y × Y →R be a continuously-differentiable function,
and let Z = U +× Y be the space of input-output pairs. For each z = (u, y) ∈Z, let Lz : Θ →R
be the function Lz(θ) = loss
 S(u; θ), y

. The two following claims hold: (i) for every z ∈Z, there
is a subset Θz ⊆Θ of full measure such that the gradient ∇Lz(θ) exists for all θ ∈Θz; (ii) when U
and Θ are bounded, there is B ∈R such that ∥∇Lz(θ)∥≤B holds for all z ∈Z and all θ ∈Θz.
Proof sketch. We adopt the theory of piecewise analytic under analytic partition (PAP) functions
[42]. They include all differentiable functions, they are closed under composition, and importantly
they include min and max functions, implying that Lz is PAP. Every PAP function admits at least
one intensional derivative, and possibly many, all coinciding with the actual derivative almost
everywhere. For a composition of PAP functions, an intensional derivative can be obtained via the
chain rule, choosing an intensional derivative for each function in the composition (and the actual
gradient for differentiable functions). Our function Lz can be expressed as a composition where
the only functions that are not everywhere-differentiable are the maximum f(x) = Ln
i=1 xi and
minimum g(x) Jn
i=1 xi of scalars xi. The maximum function f admits so-called subgradients,
and the minimum function g admits so-called supergradients, cf. [43]. We show that the super-
and sub-gradient of minimum norm are an intensional derivatives, and hence they can be used to
compute the overall intensional derivative. They are defined via the set I(x) of active-variable indices,
consisting of each index i such that f(x) = xi or g(x) = xi, respectively. Then, they are both given
by the vector (a1, . . . , an) where ai = 1/|I(x)| if i ∈I(x) and ai = 0 otherwise. Since all such
vectors consist of non-negative entries summing to one, they play no role in increasing any intensional
derivative of Lz. The other derivatives are the ones of the MLPs, which are bounded since MLPs
are continuously-differentiable and hence their derivatives are bounded on bounded domains, as it
happens in MinMax RNCs by Theorem 7. Therefore, we obtain an intensional derivative of Lz that
is bounded, and concides with ∇Lz for almost all θ ∈Θ, showing the theorem.
Remark 1 (Gradient in practice). Theorem 8 could be extended to state that our intensional derivatives
are bounded everywhere. This is relevant because they are in line with what is computed by Pytorch’s
MinMax

AutoDiff [44]. However, different computation graphs may result into different intensional derivatives,
as the chain rule for PAP function is not guaranteed to be invariant across different decompositions of
a function. Thus, we could only make a claim for the intensional derivatives obtained through the
computation graph we have considered-corresponding to sequential evaluation. We see it unlikely
that choosing a different computation graph will impact boundedness of intensional derivatives based
on super/subgradients of minimum norm, but we are not able to make a general claim at the moment.
Theorem 9 (Non-vanishing gradient). Let S be a MinMax Neuron with input space U, state space
X = R, and input functions (R, s). Let u = u1 · · · ut ∈U +, and let xu
t : X →X be the function
such that xu
t (x0) is the state of S at time t on input u starting from x0 ∈X. We have ∇xu
t (x0) = 1
for all x0 ∈(at, bt), where at = max{s(u1), . . . , s(ut)} and bt = min{R(u1), . . . , R(ut)}.
Proof sketch. The gradient of min and max functions is determined by the active variables as dis-
cussed above in the proof sketch of Theorem 8. Specifically its component for an active variable is
the reciprocal of the number of active variables. When x0 ∈(at, bt), we can see by induction on t
that xu
t−1 is the only active variable in zu
t = R(ut) ⊙xu
t−1, and in turn zu
t is the only active variable
in xu
t = zu
t ⊕s(ut). Then, by the chain rule, ∇xu
t (x0) is the product of gradients of value 1.
Corollary 3. For scalar functions R(u) = u and s(u) = −u, for every t ∈N and every constant
sequence u = (a)t
i=1 with a ∈R>0, the state gradient at time t is ∇xu
t (x0) = 1 for all x0 ∈(−a, a).
Empirical Evaluation
Our first goal is to assess the capabilities of MinMax RNCs of degree one to learn group-free tasks:
(i) we consider three benchmarks that taken together should provide a representative understanding:
Latching(n) from [45], InductionHeads(n) from [13, 46], and Sequences(n) designed by us;
(ii) we compare against the SOTA recurrent models Mamba [13], mLSTM and sLSTM [8], and
xLSTM-L [34], that include both models based on linear recurrence (Mamba, mLSTM, xLSTM-L)
and models based on non-linear recurrence (sLSTM), all having characterisation of their formal
expressivity [32, 33]; (iii) we train on sequences of length up to 512, validate up to 2048, and evaluate
on significantly longer sequences up to length 1M; with the rationale that the ability of a model to
generalise from length 512 to 1M should be reasonably indicate whether it has learned to solve the
task in general, or only its restriction to sequences of a bounded length.
Benchmarks.
Latching(n) [45] consists in returning the first element of the input sequence,
generated from a set of 20n tokens, with n that can appear first. Sequences(n) consists in matching
patterns of n consecutive events: given two sequences of token sets E1 = (E1,t)n
t=1, E2 = (E2,t)n
t=1
representing the relevant events, and token sets I1, I2 representing restart instructions, the task consists
in returning the binary pair (y1, y2) where yi = 1 iff there is a subsequence (at)n
t=1 of the input
sequence that is not interleaved by tokens of Ii and satisfies Vn
t=1(at ∈Ei,t). InductionHeads(n)
[13, 46] consists in recalling, at the second occurrence of a designated marker, the token that has
appeared right after the first occurrence of the marker; with n possible tokens to recall, the marker’s
Table 1: Evaluation accuracy (%): means of 99% CIs with maximum error margin 1.7; for
Latching(2n) and Sequences(n) the are averaged over n.

first occurrence within the first 30 steps for training, and 50 for validation and evaluation. The
benchmarks test different key abilities: Latching(n) tests the ability to store and retain information,
Sequences(n) tests sequential processing abilities, and InductionHeads(n) tests recall capabilities.
Setup.
We implement MinMax RNCs in PyTorch [47]. They have state degree one, pre-normed
layers with residual connections, initialisation following [8], and they interleave parallel and sequential
evaluation to maximise GPU usage while being able to process sequences of arbitrary length. Their
training configuration is: Adam optimiser with weight decay 1e−4, constant learning rate 1e−3, batch
size 64 (and 8 in some experiments for a closer comparison to Mamba). The other models are trained
according to the respective papers. In the case of multiple hyperparameter configurations, we evaluate
the model having the highest validation accuracy. We set a time limit of 4h for each combination
of a model and a benchmark instance. Datasets have size 20k for training, 1k for validation, and
1k for evaluation. All models are set to a comparable size and architecture: fixed for Latching(n)
and InductionHeads(n) with 2 layers and ~125k params; for Sequences(n) the same but with
n + 1 layers-all models fail to learn with fewer layers. Latching(n) and Sequences(n) are seen
as seq-to-seq tasks, averaging loss and accuracy across sequence steps. For InductionHeads(n) we
measure loss and accuracy at the recall step. Experiments are run using a single A100 GPU.
Results.
Table 1 reports the results. The only timeouts regard sLSTM’s training-the only non-
parallelisable model. On Latching(n) and Sequences(n), MinMax and Mamba always achieve
perfect validation accuracy, and xLSTM-L, mLSTM, sLSTM’s accuracy is always above 99.9, except
xLSMT-L having 0.4 on Latching(512). When evaluated, MinMax RNC still achieves perfect
accuracy, whereas the other models’s accuracy decreases significantly, as shown in Figure 2. On
InductionHeads(16), Mamba has perfect validation accuracy for batch size bs = 8 (consistent with
[13]) whereas it has 7.5 for bs = 64 Thus, for a closer comparison, we ensure that bs = 8 is included
in all trainings. MinMax and mLSTM achieve perfect validation accuracy for both batch sizes,
sLSTM has maximum validation accuracy 87.2, and xLSTM-L has 8.3. When evaluated, MinMax
has perfect accuracy, same for Mamba trained with bs = 8, whereas all other models drop to 6.2-6.7.
On InductionHeads(30) MinMax achieves perfect validation accuracy for bs = 8 and accuracy
93.6 for bs = 64, mLSTM achieves perfect validation accuracy for bs = 8, 64, Mamba reaches at
most 5.2, sLSTM at most 88.3, and xLSTM-L at most 4.7. When evaluated, MinMax trained with
bs = 8 scores perfectly, MinMax trained with bs = 64 scores 93.0, and all other models have a low
accuracy. This is the only experiment where MinMax results into a model with a validation accuracy
below pefect; notably, such validation accuracy (93.6) is closely matched by the evaluation accuracy
(93.0), showing that MinMax can length-generalise even when it has learned a perfect pattern.
Next-token prediction.
We have trained a MinMax RNC for next-token prediction following [48],
which reproduces GPT-2 on OpenWebText (OWT) [49]. The MinMax RNC has 127M parameters,
with dstate = 1, nlayers = 12, dmodel = 728, nunits = 1456. It achieves a validation loss of 3.12, the
same loss reported in [48] for the released OpenAI GPT-2 124M checkpoint when evaluated on OWT.
Conclusion
The theoretical analysis and empirical evaluation suggest that MinMax recurrence is a relevant
alternative to the existing forms of recurrence. Our future work will focus on better understanding
the effectiveness of MinMax RNCs in real-world applications, and also on the potential of MinMax
recurrence as a mechanism to integrate existing architectures in order to improve their capabilities.

A
Additional Preliminaries
We write N for the natural numbers, we write N>0 for the natural numbers exlcuding zero, we write
R for the real numbers, and we write R>0 for the positive real numbers (exluding zero).
For X a set, we write P+(X) for the set of all non-empty subsets of X.
For X a set, we write X∗for the set of all finite sequences x1, . . . , xn of elements from X. As a
special case, X∗includes the empty sequence, denoted by ε. Then, the set X+ is X∗\ {ε}.
Given i, j ∈N, we write [i..j] for the integer interval {i, . . . , j}, we write (i..j] for the integer
interval {i + 1, . . . , j}, and we write [i..j) for the integer interval {i, . . . , j −1}. Such intervals can
be empty, e.g., the interval (1..1] is empty.

Cascade Composition and Parallel Composition
We reiterate the definition of cascade and parallel composition, describing more explicitly.
Given systems S1 = ⟨X1, U, f1, x0
1, Z, h1⟩and S2 = ⟨X2, Z, f2, x0
2, Y, h2⟩, the cascade composition
S1 ⋉S2 yields the system S = ⟨X1 × X2, U, f, x0, Y, h⟩with initial state x0 = ⟨x0
1, x0
2⟩, dynamics
function f(⟨x1, x2⟩, u) = ⟨x′
1, f2(x2, h1(x1, u, x′
1))⟩for x′
1 = f1(x1, u1), and output function
h(⟨x1, x2⟩, u, ⟨x′
1, x′
2⟩) = h2(x2, h1(x1, u, x′
1), x′
2).
Given dynamics D1 = ⟨X1, U, f1⟩and D2 = ⟨X2, U × X1, f2⟩, the (serial) cascade composition
D1 ⋉D2 yields the dynamics D = ⟨X1 × X2, U, f⟩with dynamics function f(⟨x1, x2⟩, u) =
⟨f1(x1, u1), f2(x2, ⟨u, x1⟩)⟩; we also write f = f1 ⋉f2.
Given dynamics D1 = ⟨X1, U, f1⟩and D2 = ⟨X2, U, f2⟩, the parallel composition D1 ∥D2 yields
the dynamics D = ⟨X1 × X2, U, f⟩with f(⟨x1, x2⟩, u) = ⟨f1(x1, u), f2(x2, u)⟩; we also write
f = f1 ∥f2.
As both operators are associative, one can write compositions such as S1⋉· · ·⋉Sn and D1 ∥· · · ∥Dn
with no need to specify parentheses.
A.1
Semigroup and Group Theory
A (finite) semigroup is a finite set S equipped with a binary associative operator · : S × S →S, i.e.,
(a · b) · c = a · (b · c) for all a, b, c ∈S. We define S · S := {a · b | a, b ∈S}, Sn := S · Sn−1 with
S1 = S, and S+ := S∞
n=1 Sn.
A group is a semigroup G = (AG, ·G) such that (i) there exists an element e ∈AG such that, for
every a ∈AG, we have e ·G a = g ·G e = a; then e is unique and it is called the identity element of G;
(ii) for every a ∈AG, there exists an element b ∈AG such that a ·G b = b ·G a = e; then b is unique
for a, it is called its inverse, and it is denoted by a−1.
A semigroup S′ = (AS′, ·S′) is a subsemigroup of a semigroup S = (AS, ·S) if AS′ ⊆AS. A group
G′ = (AG′, ·G′) is a subgroup of a group G = (AG, ·G) if AG′ ⊆AG.
A transformation on a set X is a function f : X →X.
A transformation semigroup is a pair (X, T) where X is a set, T is a set of transformations on X,
and (T, ◦) is a semigroup with ◦the standard function composition; it is a transformation group if
(T, ◦) is a group.
A permutation on a set X is a bijective transformation on X.
A permutation group over a finite set X is a transformation group (X, P) where P is a set of
permutations over X; and its degree is |X|.
The symmetric group over a finite set X, written Sym(X), is the transformation group (X, P) where
P is the set of all permutations over X; and the degree of Sym(X) is |X|. Every permutation group
on X is a subgroup of Sym(X).
An isomorphism between two semigroups S1 = (A1, ·1) and S2 = (A2, ·2) is a bijection ψ : A1 →
A2 such that ψ(a) ·2 ψ(a′) = ψ(a ·1 a′) for all a, a′ ∈A1.
Theorem 10 (Cayley). Every group G = (A, ·) is isomorphic to a subgroup of Sym(A).
A.2
Algebraic Automata Theory
Algebraic Automata Theory (AAT), cf. [37, 38, 50, 51], offers a refined perspective on automata,
seeing an automaton not as a transition system (a graph where one moves from a node to another
according to a given input), but instead as a device that manipulates elements of an underlying set by
applying transformations selected by the inputs. As as result, it connects automata to semigroup and
group theory, allowing for advanced characterisations automata.
An automaton is a system A = ⟨Q, Σ, δ, q0, Γ, θ⟩where Q, Σ, Γ are finite spaces under the discrete
metric. The standard terminology differs from the one of dynamical systems as Σ is called input
alphabet, δ is called transition function, Γ is called output alphabet, and the tuple D = ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩is
called a semiautomaton.
For σ ∈Σ, the state transformation δσ of D is the function δσ : Q →Q given by δσ(q) = δ(q, σ).
Semiautomaton D is an identity-reset semiautomaton if each transformation δσ is the identity function
or a constant function, and it is a permutation semiautomaton if each transformation δσ is a bijection.
The transition monoid of D is the transformation monoid M(D) = (X, ◦) where
X = {id} ∪{δσ1 ◦· · · ◦δσn | σ1, . . . , σn ∈Σ}.
For G = ⟨G, γ⟩a finite group with elements G and operator γ : G × G →G, the G-semiautomaton
is AG = ⟨G, G, γ⟩.
A Mealy machine is a tuple M = ⟨Q, Σ, δ, Γ, θ⟩where DM = ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩is a semiautomaton, Γ is
an output alphabet, and θ : Q × Σ →Γ is an output function. It defines the mapping Q × Σ+ →Γ
given by
M(q, σ1 · · · σn) = θ
 DM(q, σ1 · · · σn), σn

.
Given an automaton A = ⟨Q, Σ, δ, q0, Γ, θ⟩, the associated Mealy machine MA = ⟨Q, Σ, δ, Γ, θ⟩is
obtained by dropping the initial state from A.
Given a semiautomaton DA = ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩, its canonical Mealy machine is
M(D) := ⟨Q, Σ, δ, Γ, θ⟩,
where Γ := Q × Σ, and θ := id.
Definition 4 (Assisgnments and realisations; Definitions 1.14 and 1.15 of [38]). If M
=
⟨Q, Σ, δ, Γ, θ⟩and M ′ = ⟨Q′, Σ′, δ′, Γ′, θ′⟩are Mealy Machines, then the triple (α, ι, ζ) is called an
assignment of M into M ′ when the functions
α : Q →P+(Q′),
ι : Σ →Σ′,
ζ : Γ′ →Γ,
satisfy the two conditions below for every q ∈Q, every q′ ∈α(q), and every σ ∈Σ.
I)

If an assignment of M into M ′ exists, then we say that M ′ is a realisation of M or, equivalently, that
M ′ realises M.
Fact 1 (§1.3 of [38]). If M is a realisation of M ′ and M ′ is a realisation of M ′′, then M realies M ′′.
Theorem 11 (Theorem 1.5 in §1.3 of [38]). If M ′ = ⟨Q′, Σ′, δ′, Γ′, θ′⟩is a realisation of M =
⟨Q, Σ, δ, Γ, θ⟩through an assignment (α, ι, ζ), then for all q0 ∈Q, w := w[1..ℓ] ∈Σ+, and
q′

Theorem 12 (Krohn-Rhodes, cf. Theorem 7.9 in [38]). Let A = ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩be a semiautomaton,
and let G be the set of simple groups that are factors of a subgroup of the transition monoid of A.
Then, A is realised by a cascade A1 ⋉· · · ⋉Am where each Ai is either a two-state identity-reset
semiautomaton or a G-semiautomaton for some G ∈G.
Corollary 4. Let A = ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩be a semiautomaton. Then, A is realised by a cascade A1⋉· · ·⋉Am
where each Ai is an identity-reset or a permutation semiautomaton.
Proof. Implies by the Krohn-Rhodes theorem since the state transformations of any G-semiautomaton
AG = ⟨G, G, γ⟩are bijections. In fact, any transformation is given by γg(g′) = γ(g′, g), which is
invertible since every group element admits an inverse element, and hence it is a bijection.
A.3
Metric Automata Theory
Metric Automata Theory (MAT) [33] extends the applicability of AAT to arbitrary dynamical systems,
with automata as a special case. We introduce the relevant notions.
A path in X from a to b is a continuous map γ : [0, 1] →X such that γ(0) = a and γ(1) = b. We
can define a relation ∼X, where a ∼X b when there is a path in X from a to b. This relation is
an equivalence, partitioning X into equivalence classes [x]∼X = {x′ ∈X | x′ ∼X x} for x ∈X,
called (path-connected) components. For space X, we denote the set of its equivalence classes by X.
Path-connectedness is preserved by every continuous function f : X →Y , and hence the function
f : X →Y with f(x) = f(x) is well-defined. An open ball BX(x, r) and closed ball BX(x, r) at
x ∈X of radius r ≥0 in a metric space ⟨X, d⟩is the set of points in X with distance δ < r and
δ ≤r from x, respectively:
BX(x, r) := {y ∈X | d(x, y) < r},
BX(x, r) := {y ∈X | d(x, y) ≤r}.
A metric space X is η-finite if it is a finite union of compact, path-connected components. The
continuous image of an η-finite space is η-finite (Lemma 18 of [33]). The Cartesian-product X × Y
space of η-finite spaces is η-finite. The η-components of X × Y are the products η-components of X
and η-components of Y (Lemma 19 of [33]).
Lemma 1 (Lemma 22 of [33]). Let X be an η-finite space and Σ a finite alphabet. Then a function
f : X →Σ is continuous if and only if it is constant on the η-components of X.
Dynamics D = ⟨X, U, f⟩and system S = ⟨X, U, f, x0, Y, h⟩are η-finite if X and U are
η-finite; in such case, the semiautomaton Cη(D) := ⟨X, U, f⟩and the automaton Cη(S) :=
⟨X, U, f, [x0]∼X, Y , h⟩are well-defined and they are called the η-canonical semiautomaton of
D and the η-canonical automaton of S, respectively.
Definition 5 (Definition 1 of [33]). Given alphabets Σ and Γ, and continuous functions enc : Σ →U
and dec : Y →Γ, we say that a system S implements a function F : Σ+ →Γ, with encoder enc
and decoder dec, if F(w) = dec
 S(enc(w))

, for every w ∈Σ+, where enc(w) ∈U + applies enc
element-wise. We also say that S can-implement F if it implements F for some choice of enc and
dec. When Γ = {0, 1}, we say that S recognises a language L if it implements its indicator function
IL, and that S can-recognise L if it can-implement its characteristic function IL.
Theorem 13 (Theorem 1 of [33]). An η-finite system S can-implement the same functions as its
canonical automaton, which are necessarily regular.
Definition 6 (Definition 23 of [33]). Given η-finite dynamics D, D′, dynamics D′ are a realisation
of D if M(Cη(D′)) is a realisation of M(Cη(D)) of D. Given automata A, A′, automaton A′ is a
realisation of A if the associated machine MA′ is a realisation of of the associated machine MA via
an assignment (α, ι, ζ), and the respective initial states x′
0, x0 are such that x′
0 ∈α(x0). Given an
η-finite system S′ and a system S, system S′ is a realisation of S if Cη(S′) is a realisation of Cη(S).
■
Lemma 2 (Lemma 29 of [33]). Suppose that semiautomaton D′ is a realisation of semiautomaton D.
Then (i) for any machine M with dynamics D, the canonical machine M(D′) of D′ is a realisation
of M; (ii) for any automaton A with dynamics D, an initial state can be picked for M(D′) such that
the resulting automaton is a realisation of A.
Theorem 14 (Theorem 30 of [33]). Let S and S′ be η-finite systems. If Cη(S′) is a realisation of
Cη(S), then S′ can implement all the functions that S can implement.
Theorem 15 (Theorem 31 of [33]). Let D, D′ be η-finite dynamics. Suppose that D′ is a realisation
of D. Then any function implemented by a system with dynamics D can be implemented by some
system S′ with dynamics D′ and an identity output function.
In the above Theorem 15, the remark on the identity output function is not present in the original
statement of the theorem, but it is clear from its proof.
A.3.1
Aperiodicity and eta-convergence
Definition 7 (Convergence, Definition 31 [33]). For a η-finite space X, we say a sequence (xn)n≥1
with xn ∈X η-converges in X, if eventually all its terms lie in the same η-component of X.
Definition 8 (Aperiodicity, Definition 32 [33]). We say that η-finite dynamics D = ⟨X, U, f⟩are
aperiodic if, for every x0 ∈X and every input sequence (un)n≥1 that is η-convergent in U, we have
that the corresponding state sequence (xn)n≥1 is η-convergent in X.
Theorem 16 (Theorem 50 of [33]). η-finite dynamics are aperiodic if and only if their η-canonical
semiautomaton is group-free.
A.4
Models of Computation
The complexity results are established considering the following models of computation.
Random Access Machines.
The sequential complexity results are proven under the assumptions of
Random-Access Machine (RAM) on real-valued arithmetic, cf. [52]. A RAM has a finite number of
registers and, in one computation step, it may perform one of the following operations:
• write to a register the scalar value a ◦b, where a, b are scalar values read from some registers, for
any operation ◦∈{+, −, ×, ÷, ⊙, ⊕} and b ̸= 0 in the case of division;
• write to a register the scalar value ⌈a⌉or ⌊a⌋for a a scalar value read from a register;
• compare the value of two registers and branch accordingly.
The instructions in the pseudocode we use are in line with RAM, while providing a higher-level
specification language (e.g., registers correspond to variables and entries of a tensor, branching is
involved in if-then-else and in for loops). Importantly, in our pseudocode we only use the elementary
arithmetic operations mentioned above, whose cost is assumed to be 1.
Parallel Random Access Machines.
The parallel complexity results are proven considering
Parallel RAM on real-valued arithmetic with Concurrent Read and Exclusive Write (PRAM-CREW),
cf. [31]. A PRAM-CREW has an arbitrary number of processors that carry out the computation
proceeding by rounds. In each round, the processors simultaneously perform one of the operations
described above for the RAM model, under the condition that two processors never write to the
same register in the same round. The work is the total number of operations performed by the
processors, and the depth is the number of rounds. The instructions in our pseudocode are in line
with the PRAM-CREW, while providing a higher-level specification language. Compared to the
sequential case, we now have the ‘parallel for’ instruction, which allows us to describe simultaneous
computations carried out by the processors. Brent’s Theorem translates work and depth to execution
time on PRAM-CREW.
Theorem 17 (Brent’s Theorem, cf. Theorem 25.1 in [31]). Any algorithm having work W and depth
D can be executed in O(W/P + D) steps in the CREW-PRAM model.
A.5
Computation Problems
Definition 9. A computation problem over R is a triple ⟨I, O, f⟩where I is a set of tuples of tensors
called valid inputs or simply inputs, O is a set of tuples of tensors called possible outputs or simply
outputs, f : I →O is a function that maps every input element x ∈I to an output element f(x) ∈O
called the expected output for input x. The size of an input x ∈I is the number of scalar values in the
tensors of x.
Definition 10. A program P solves a computation problem ⟨I, O, f, s⟩if, for every input x ∈I, we
have that x is a valid input for program P and the output P(x) of the program equals f(x).
Definition 11. For T : N →N a monotonic function, a sequential program P solves a computation
problem ⟨I, O, f⟩in time T if, for every input x ∈I, we have that x is a valid input for P, the the
output P(x) of the program equals f(x), and the execution time of P on input x is at most T(n) for
n the size of input x.
Definition 12. For W, D : N →N monotonic functions, a parallel program P solves a computation
problem ⟨I, O, f⟩with depth D and work W if, for every input x ∈I, we have that x is a valid input
for P, the output of the program P(x) equals f(x), the depth of the execution of P on x is at most
D(n) for n the size x, and the work of the execution of P on x is at most W(n).
Definition 13. For T, P : N →N monotonic functions, a parallel program P solves a computation
problem ⟨I, O, f⟩in time T having P processors available if, for every input x ∈I, we have that x
is a valid input for P, the output of the program P(x) equals f(x), and the execution time of program
P on input x having P(n) processors available is at most T(n) for n is the size of input x.
Definition 14. We say that a computation problem can be solved sequentially in time T if there exists
a sequential program that solves it in time T. We say that a computation problem can be solved in
parallel with work W and depth D if there exists a parallel program that solves it with work W and
depth D. We say that a computation problem can be solved in parallel in time T having P processors
available if there exists a parallel program that solves it in time T having P processors available.
B
Parametric Systems
We first generalise dynamical systems and related notions to the parametric case.
B.1
Paramtric Dynamical Systems
Parametric dynamics are a tuple D = ⟨X, U, f; Θ⟩where X, U, Θ are metric spaces, and f :
X × U × Θ →X is a continuous function. The dynamics Dθ obtained by fixing the parameters to
θ ∈Θ are Dθ = ⟨X, U, fθ⟩where fθ(x, u, x′) := f(x, u, x′; θ).
A parametric (dynamical) system is a tuple S = ⟨X, U, f, xinit, Y, h; Θ⟩where X, U, Y, Θ are metric
spaces, f : X × U × Θ →X and h : X × U × Θ× X →Y are continuous functions, and xinit ∈X
is called the initial state of the system. The tuple D = ⟨X, U, f; Θ⟩are the parametric dynamics of
S. The system Sθ obtained by fixing the parameters to θ ∈Θ is Sθ = ⟨X, U, fθ, xinit, Y, hθ⟩where
fθ(x, u, x′) := f(x, u, x′; θ) and hθ(x, u, x′) := h(x, u, x′; θ).
B.1.1
State sequence
Let us consider dynamics D = ⟨X, U, f; Θ⟩, an input sequence u = u1, . . . , uT ∈U ∗, and a value
θ ∈Θ for the parameters.
For every state x0 ∈X, dynamics D = ⟨X, U, f; Θ⟩induce the sequence of states
x0, x1, . . . , xT
where xt = f(xt−1, ut; θ) is the state of D at time t ∈[1..T], given that the state at time 0 is x0.
The function defined by D is the function D : X × U ∗× Θ →X such that D(x0, u; θ) = xT ; in
particular, we have D(x0, u; θ) = x0 when u is the empty sequence.
When D are the dynamics of a system S, and x0 is the initial state xinit of S, then xt is the state of S
at time t ∈[1..T].
B.1.2
Output sequence
Let us consider a system S = ⟨X, U, f, xinit, Y, h; Θ⟩, an input sequence u = u1, . . . , uT ∈U ∗, and
a value θ ∈Θ for the parameters.
The output of S at time t ∈[1..T] is yt = h(xt−1, ut, xt; θ), where xt−1 and xt are the states of S at
time t −1 and t, respectively. The function defined by S is the function S : U + × Θ →Y such that
S(u; θ) = yT .
B.2
Composition of dynamics
Cascade composition of dynamics.
Given parametric dynamics
D1 = ⟨X1, U, f1; Θ1⟩,
D2 = ⟨X2, U × X1, f2; Θ2⟩,
their (serial) cascade composition yields the dynamics
D1 ⋉D2 := ⟨X1 × X2, U, f; Θ1 × Θ2⟩
where the dynamics function is
f(⟨x1, x2⟩, u; θ1, θ2) :=

f1(x1, u1; θ1), f2(x2, ⟨u, x1⟩; θ2)

.
We also write f = f1 ⋉f2.
Parallel composition of dynamics.
Given parametric dynamics
D1 = ⟨X1, U, f1; Θ1⟩,
D2 = ⟨X2, U, f2; Θ2⟩,
their parallel composition yields the parametric dynamics
D1 ∥D2 := ⟨X1 × X2, U, f; Θ1 × Θ2⟩
with dynamics function
f(⟨x1, x2⟩, u; θ1, θ2) :=

f1(x1, u; θ1), f2(x2, u; θ2)

.
We also write f = f1 ∥f2.
Associativity.
As both operators are associative, one can write compositions such as D1 ⋉· · ·⋉Dn
and D1 ∥· · · ∥Dn with no need to specify parentheses.
B.3
Cascade composition of systems
Given parametric systems
S1 = ⟨X1, U, f1, x0
1, Z, h1; Θ1⟩,
S2 = ⟨X2, Z, f2, x0
2, Y, h2; Θ2⟩,
their cascade composition yields the parametric system
S1 ⋉S2 :=

X1 × X2, U, f, ⟨x0
1, x0
2⟩, Y, h; Θ1 × Θ2

,
where the dynamics function is
f
 ⟨x1, x2⟩, u; θ1, θ2
 := ⟨x′
1, f2(x2, z; θ2)⟩
for z := h1(x1, u, x′
1; θ1) and x′
1 := f1(x1, u1; θ1),
and the output function is
h
 ⟨x1, x2⟩, u, ⟨x′
1, x′
2⟩; θ1, θ2
 := h2(x2, z, x′
2; θ2)
for z := h1(x1, u, x′
1; θ1).
Associativity.
As the cascade composition operator is associative, one can write compositions such
as S1 ⋉· · · ⋉Sn with no need to specify parentheses.
C
Additional Notions for MinMax RNCs
C.1
MinMax Recurrence Operator
We introduce the following notation for the MinMax recurrence.
Definition 15. MinMax Recurrence of dimension n ∈N is the function
MinMaxRec :
 Rn × Rn×n × Rn
→Rn,
MinMaxRec(x, A, b) :=
 A ⊗x

⊕b.
C.2
Parametric MinMax RNCs
Definition 16. A parametric MinMax Recurrent Unit is defined as parametric dynamics D =
⟨X, U, f; Θ⟩with state space X ⊆Rn, input space U ⊆Rm, parameter space Θ ⊆Rp, and
dynamics function
f(x, u; θ) =
 R(u; θ) ⊗x

⊕s(u; θ),
where the functions R : U × Θ →Rn×n and s : U × Θ →Rn are referred to as reset function
and set function, respectively, and also as input functions jointly. They can be specified by writing
D = ⟨X, U, f; Θ | R, s⟩.
Definition 17. A parametric MinMax Recurrent Layer is a parametric system whose dynamics are
given by the parallel composition of parametric MinMax Recurrent Units. A parametric MinMax
Recurrent Cascade is a parametric system given by the cascade composition of parametric MinMax
Recurrent Layers.
Definition 18. A parametric MinMax Recurrent Neural Cascade (MinMax RNC) is a parametric
MinMax Recurrent Cascade where the input and output functions of its layers are MLPs.
C.3
Floating-point MinMax RNCs
We first discuss the background, and then define floating-point MinMax RNCs.
C.3.1
Floating-point MLPs
We adopt all definitions from [53]. In particular, the definition of floating-point domain and operations
they give in §2.1, and the definition of floating-point neural networks they give in §2.2. We will refer
to their floating-point neural networks as F-MLPs. By their Corollary 3.8 and Corollary 3.11, F-MLPs
can represent all functions over Fn. Note that F-MLPs are not simply MLPs obtained by restricting
ordinary MLPs to the floating-point subset of their real-valued domain. Instead, F-MLPs are the
composition of elementary functions that are provided by the considered floating-point arithmetic.
C.3.2
Floating-point MinMax RNCs
We next introduce a variant of MinMax Neurons using floating-point arithmetic over a floating-point
domain F.
Definition 19. For F a floating-point domain, we define a floating-point MinMax Recurrent Unit
over F with input functions R : U →Fn×n and s : U →Fn as dynamics D = ⟨X, U, f⟩having
state space X ⊆Fn, input space U ⊆Fm, and dynamics function:
f(x, u) =
 R(u) ⊗x

⊕s(u).
We call R the reset function and s the set function, and we specify them as D = ⟨X, U, f | R, s⟩.
Then, dynamics D are a floating-point MinMax Recurrent Neuron if R and s are F-MLPs.
■
We define floating-point MinMax RNCs.
Definition 20. For F a floating-point domain, a floating-point MinMax Recurrent Cascade over F is
a cascade composition S1 ⋉· · · ⋉Sn where each Si is a floating-point MinMax Recurrent Layer over
F, defined as a system having dynamics Di given by a parallel composition Di,1 ∥· · · ∥Di,ni of
floating-point MinMax Recurrent Units over F. A floating-point MinMax Recurrent Neural Cascade
(MinMax RNC) over F is a MinMax Recurrent Cascade over F where (i) each Unit Di,j is a MinMax
Recurrent Neuron over F, and (ii) the output function of each Layer Si is an F-MLP. The state degree
of S is the maximum state dimension of any Di,j.
■
We observe that the only functions a floating-point MinMax RNC introduces, in addition to the ones
from the F-MLPs, are the min and max functions, which are natively floating-point functions when
evaluated on F, since they simply return values of F.
D
Expressivity Proofs
In this appendix we provide full proofs of the results of Section 4.
• Section D.1 presents preliminary results.
• Subsection D.1.1 develops the preliminary results for characterising the state degree of MinMax
Neurons in terms of the permutation degree of a semiautomaton.
• Subsections D.1.2, D.1.3 jointly provide preliminary results regarding the cascading dynamics
while preserving the characteristics we are interested in to show results later.
• Section D.2 proves the theorems of Section 4.1, also making use of the preliminary results from
Subsection D.1.1.
• Section D.3 focuses on realising casacades of semiautomata using cascades of MinMax Neurons,
also making use of the preliminary results from Subsections D.1.2, D.1.3.
• Section D.4 proves the main expressivity theorem (Theorem 1), relying on all of the above.
Additionally, we prove that the expressivity results hold seamlessly for floating-point MinMax RNCs
(Theorem 26 and Corollary 5).
D.1
Auxiliary Results
D.1.1
Permutation Degree
We provide the more formally-precise version of Definition 3.
Definition 21 (See Def. 3). The minimal faithful permutation degree of a group G, written µ(G), is
the smallest number n such that there is an isomorphism between G and a subgroup of the symmetric
group Sym([1..n]). The degree of a permutation semiautomaton is the degree of its transition monoid
(which is a group). The degree of a regular function F is the maximum between one and the degree
of any simple group that is a factor of a subgroup of the transition monoid of the (semiautomaton of)
the canonical automaton of F.
Theorem 18. Let G be a group having minimal faithful permutation degree n, and let A = ⟨G, G, δ⟩
be the semiautomaton with δ(x, a) = xa. There exists φ : G →Sym([1..n]) such that A is realised
by the semiautomaton A′ = ⟨[1..n]n, G, δ′⟩with δ′(⟨x1, . . . , xn⟩, g) = ⟨xπ(1), . . . , xπ(n)⟩for π the
permutation π = φ(g).
Proof. For each g ∈G, let
δg : G →G,
δg(x) = xg,
Then, the transition group of A is GA = {δg | g ∈G}.
Since G has minimal faithful permutation degree equal to n, by Cayley’s theorem (reported as
Theorem 10 in Section A.1), there exists an isomorphism α : G →H between G and a subgroup H
of the symmetric group Sym([1..n]).
Let us define the semiautomaton A′ = (H, G, δ′) where
δ′(h, g) = hα(g)
(h ∈H, g ∈G).
Noting that the inverse function α−1 : H →G is well-defined since α is bijective, we define the
function ζ : H × G →G × G as ζ(h, g) = ⟨α−1(h), g⟩. Then we show that the tuple (α, id, ζ) is
an assignment of M(A) into M(A′).
Let x, σ ∈G. It suffices to show
(I) δ′(α(x), σ) = α(δ(x, σ)),
(II) ζ(α(x), σ) = ⟨x, σ⟩.
We have that (I) holds since
δ′(α(x), σ) = α(x)α(σ)
= α(xσ)
= α(δ(x, σ)).
We have that (II) holds since
ζ(α(x), σ) = ⟨α−1(α(x)), σ⟩
= ⟨x, σ⟩.
Now, let us define the semiautomaton A′′ = ([1..n]n, G, δ′′) where
δ′′(⟨i1, . . . , in⟩, g) := ⟨iπ(1), . . . , iπ(n)⟩
for π = α(g).
We define the function α′ : H →[1..n]n as α′(h) = ⟨h(1), . . . , h(n)⟩, and we define the function
ζ′ : [1..n]n ×G →G×G as ζ′(⟨i1, . . . , in⟩, g) := ⟨π, g⟩for π : [1..n] →[1..n] such that π(j) = ij.
Then we show that the tuple (α′, id, ζ′) is an assignment of M(A′) into M(A′′).
Let h ∈H, g ∈G. It suffices to show
(I) δ′′(α′(h), g) = α′(δ′(h, g)),
(II) ζ′(α′(g), g) = ⟨h, g⟩.
We have that (I) holds since
δ′′(α′(h), g) = δ′′(⟨h(1), . . . , h(n)⟩, g)
= ⟨h(π(1)), . . . , h(π(n))⟩
for π = α(g)
= ⟨(hπ)(1), . . . , (hπ)(n)⟩
= α′(hπ)
= α′(hα(g))
α′ δ′(h, g)

.
We have that (II) holds since
ζ′(α′(h), g) = ζ′(⟨h(1), . . . , h(n)⟩, g)
= ζ′(π, g)
for π such that π(i) = h(i) for all i, but then π = h, and hence ζ′(α′(h), g) = ζ′(h, g) as required.
Overall, we have shown that A′′ realises A′, that in turn realises A. Thus, by transitivity, we conclude
that A′′ realises A. Furthermore, we have that φ := α′ ◦α is a function G →Sym([1..n]). Thus, φ
and A′′ witness the claim of the theorem.
Theorem 19. Let A = ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩be a permutation semiautomaton of degree n. There exists
φ : Σ →Sym([1..n]) such that A is realised by the semiautomaton A′ = ⟨[1..n]n, Σ, δ′⟩with
δ′(⟨x1, . . . , xn⟩, g) = ⟨xπ(1), . . . , xπ(n)⟩for π the permutation π = φ(σ).
Proof. The transition monoid M(A) = (G, ◦) of A is a group of degree n. By Theorem 18 there
is φ′ : G →Sym([1..n]) such that the M(A)-semiautomaton is realised by the semiautomaton
A′ = ⟨[1..n]n, G, δ′⟩with δ′(⟨x1, . . . , xn⟩, g) = ⟨xπ(1), . . . , xπ(n)⟩for π the permutation π′ = φ(g).
Then A′ realises A since it realises the M(A)-semiautomaton, which realises A since it has a superset
of its transformations. Finally, φ := ψ ◦φ′ where ψ(σ) := δσ ∈G.
D.1.2
Composition with an input function
Definition 22. Dynamics D and D′ are strongly equivalent if Cη(D) = Cη(D′) (which also implies
X = X′ and U = U ′).
Definition 23. Given dynamics D = ⟨X, U, f⟩and a function φ : V →U, the composition φ ◦D
yields the dynamics Dφ = ⟨X, V, fφ⟩with fφ(x, v) = f(x, φ(v)).
Definition 24. A class of functions Ψ satifies the universal approximation property if, for every
continuous function f : X ⊆Rm →Rn with X compact and for every ϵ > 0, class Ψ contains a
function ˆf : X →Rn such that ∥f(x) −ˆf(x)∥≤ϵ for every x ∈X.
Proposition 1. Let Ψ be a class of functions satisfying the universal approximation property, let
D be η-finite dynamics with input space U, let U ′ be an η-finite space, and let φ : U ′ →U be a
continuous function. Furthermore, suppose that each η-component U η of U satisfies ¯BΩU (u) ⊆U η
for some u ∈U. Then, the dynamics φ ◦D admit strongly equivalent dynamics ψ ◦D for some
ψ ∈Ψ.
Proof. Let D = ⟨X, U, f⟩. Then, naming Dφ := φ◦D, we have Dφ = ⟨X, U ′, fφ⟩with fφ(x, u′) =
f(x, φ(u′))
Let U η
1 , . . . , U η
n be the η-components of U.
By assumption, there exist u1, . . . , un such that
B(ui, ϵ) ⊆U η
i for every i ∈[1..n]. Let φ′ : U ′ →{u1, . . . , un} be the function defined as
φ′(u′) = ui for i ∈[1..n] such that φ(u′) ∈U η
i . Fitst, we have that φ′ is well-defined since U is
partitioned by its η-components. Second, we have φ′ = φ since φ′(u′) = φ′(u′) and φ′(u′) ∈φ(u′)
by construction. Third, we have that φ′ is continuous; in fact, by continuity of φ, we have that
u′
1 ∼U ′ u′
2 implies φ(u′
1) ∼U φ(u′
2), hence φ′ is constant on the η-components of U ′, and hence φ′
is continuous by Lemma 22 of [33].
Then, since we have shown that φ′ is continuous, there exists ψ ∈Ψ that is an ϵ-approximation of φ′.
Letting Dψ := ψ ◦D, we have Dψ = ⟨X, U ′, fψ⟩with fψ(x, u′) = f(x, ψ(u′)).
Since Dφ and Dψ have the same state and input space, it suffices to show fφ = fψ.
First we show ψ = φ. Namely, letting u′ ∈U ′, we show that ψ(u′) = φ(u′). First, by the
approximation assumption, we have
ψ(u′) ∈¯BΩU
 φ′(u′), ϵ

⊆φ′(u′) = φ′(u′) = φ(u′),
with the last equality argued above. Hence ψ(u′) ∈φ(u′), which implies ψ(u′) = φ(u′). Then,
ψ(u′) = ψ(u′) = φ(u′),
which concludes the proof of the claim ψ = φ.
Finally we show fφ = fψ as required. By the two following chains of equalities,
fφ(x, u′) = fφ(x, u′) = f(x, φ(u′)) = f(x, φ(u′)),
fψ(x, u′) = fψ(x, u′) = f(x, ψ(u′)) = f(x, ψ(u′)),
we conclude fφ = fψ since we have shown above φ = ψ.
Remark 2. Under strong equivalence, we can replace D with D′ anywhere, e.g., in a cascade.
D.1.3
Cascades

Definition 25. Dynamics D and D′ are equivalent if (i) for every system S with dynamics D, there
is a system S′ with dynamics D′ such that S and S′ can implement the same functions, and (ii) for
every system S′ with dynamics D′, there is a system S with dynamics D such that S and S′ can
implement the same functions.
Proposition 2. Given η-finite dynamics D and D′, if Cη(D) = Cη(D′), then D and D′ are equivalent.
Proof. Immediate by Theorem 31 of [33].
Lemma 5. Let Φ be a class of functions satisfying the universal approximation property. Let us
consider a cascade
C =
 ϕ1,1 ◦D1,1 ∥· · · ∥ϕ1,m1 ◦D1,m1

⋉· · · ⋉
 ϕd,1 ◦Dd,1 ∥· · · ∥ϕd,md ◦Dd,md

where each Di,j is over R, it is η-finite, and it has an input space Ui such that every η-component
U η of Ui satisfies ¯BΩUi(u, ϵ) ⊆U η for some u ∈U η and some ϵ > 0. Then, there exist ˆϕi,j ∈Φ
such that the cascade
ˆC =
 ˆϕ1,1 ◦D1,1 ∥· · · ∥ˆϕ1,m1 ◦D1,m1

⋉· · · ⋉
 ˆϕd,1 ◦Dd,1 ∥· · · ∥ˆϕd,md ◦Dd,md

Proof. Let i ∈[1..d] and let j ∈[1..mi]. Let Ui be the input space of Di,j, and let U η
i,1, . . . , U η
i,ni be
the η-components of Ui. By assumption, for each ℓ∈[1..ni], there are ˜ui,ℓ∈U η
i,ℓand ϵ > 0 such that
¯BΩUi(˜ui,ℓ, ϵ) ⊆U η

the same holds for the entire cascade; namely, the η-canonical semiautomaton Cη( ˆC) coincides with
the η-canonical semiautomaton Cη(C). Then the result follows immediately by Proposition 2.
D.2
Realising semiautomata using single MinMax Neurons
In this section we first provide an additional result and then provide full proofs of the results of
Section 4.1. However, here we adopt a different execution of the proofs, where we first show the
existence of MinMax Units that allow for choosing states and inputs in a flexible way, showing tha
the input functions can be approximated, but replacing them with MLPs at a later step.
The next theorem is an additional result, showing that a single MinMax Recurrent Unit can realise
any semiautomaton. The idea is to represent the transition matrix of the semiautomaton.
Lemma 6. Let A be a semiautomaton having n states and m inputs, let ϵ ≥0, let x0, x1 ∈R
with (x1 −x0) > 2ϵ, and let U ⊆R be the union of m pair-wise disjoint closed intervals. There
exist continuous functions R : U →{x0, x1}n×n and s : U →{x0, x1}n such that semiautomaton
A can be realised by the η-finite MinMax Recurrent Unit D = ⟨Xn, U, f | R, s⟩where X =
[x0 −ϵ, x0 + ϵ] ∪[x1 −ϵ, x1 + ϵ]; furthermore, for every ϵ-approximations ( ˆR, ˆs) of (R, s), the
MinMax Recurrent Unit ˆD = ⟨Xn, U, f | ˆR, ˆs⟩is strongly equivalent to D.
Proof. Let A = ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩be a semiautomaton with states Q = {q1, . . . , qn} and input alphabet
Σ = {σ1, . . . , σm}.
Construction.
We define the MinMax Recurrent Unit D = ⟨Xn, U, f | R, s⟩.
Let U1, . . . , Um ⊆R be the pair-wise disjount closed intervals such that U = Sm
i=1 Ui.
We define the reset function R : U →{x0, x1}n×n and the set function s : U →{x0, x1}n. Let
∆:= x1 −x0. For each i ∈[1..m], let Ti ∈{0, 1}n×n be the matrix such that (Ti)j,k = 1 iff
δ(qk, σi) = qj. For each i ∈[1..m], let ai : U →{0, ∆} be the function defined as ai(u) = ∆iff
u ∈Ui. Note that the functions a1, . . . , am are continuous by Lemma 1 since they are constant over
η- components of U. Then,
R(u) := x0 · 1n,n +
m
X
i=1
ai(u) · Ti,
s(u) := x0 · 1n.
Note that function R is continuous since it is the composition of continuous functions, and function s
is continuous since it is constant. This completes the construction of the MinMax Recurrent Unit.
Next we prove the claims.
η-finiteness.
The MinMax Recurrent Unit D is η-finite since U is a finite union of closed intervals,
and Xn is the cross-product of a finite union of closed intervals.
Realisation.
To show that D can realise A, it suffices to show that M(Cη(D)) is a realisation of
M(A). Note that M(A) = ⟨Q, Σ, δ, Γ, θ⟩where Γ = Q × Σ and θ = id.
For each i ∈[1..m], let us choose an arbitrary ui ∈Ui. The construction of D allows us to easily
derive that its canonical semiautomaton has the form Cη(D) = ⟨X

Then M(Cη(D)) = ⟨X

since ι is a bijection.

Strong equivalence of approximations.
For arbitrary x ∈Xn and u ∈U, it suffices to show
f(x, u) = ˆf(x, u). First we show R(u) = ˆR(u) and s(u) = ˆs(u). For every ℓ, p ∈[1..n], we have
(R(u))ℓ,p = ( ˆR(u))ℓ,p since (R(u))ℓ,p ∈{x0, x1}, the intervals [x0 −ϵ, x0 + ϵ] and [x1 −ϵ, x1 + ϵ]
are path-connected components, and (R(u))ℓ,p = xi implies ( ˆR(u))ℓ,p ∈[xi −ϵ, xi + ϵ]. Similarly,
for every ℓ∈[1..n], we have (s(u))ℓ= (ˆs(u))ℓsince (s(u))ℓ= x0, the interval [x0 −ϵ, x0 + ϵ]
is a η-component, and (s(u))ℓ= x0 implies (ˆs(u))ℓ∈[x0 −ϵ, x0 + ϵ]. Then, recalling the nota-
tion for MinMax recurrence (Definition 15), f(x, u) = f(x, u) = MinMaxRec(x, R(u), s(u)) and
ˆf(x, u) = ˆf(x, u) = MinMaxRec(x, ˆR(u), ˆs(u)). Having R(u) = ˆR(u) and s(u) = ˆs(u), we ob-
tain MinMaxRec(x, ˆR(u), ˆs(u)) = MinMaxRec(x, R(u), s(u)) by continuity of the MinMaxRec
function, since the continuous image of path-connected components is path-connected. Therefore,
f(x, u) = ˆf(x, u) as required.
The next lemma is the full version of Theorem 2 from Section 4.1, except that we postpone replacing
the input functions with MLPs.
Lemma 7. Let A be an identity-reset semiautomaton having n states and m inputs, let ϵ ≥0, let
x1, . . . , xn ∈R with |xi −xj| > 2ϵ for all i ̸= j, and let U ⊆R be the union of m pair-wise
disjoint closed intervals. There exist continuous functions R, s : U →{x1, . . . , xn} such that
semiautomaton A can be realised by the η-finite MinMax Recurrent Unit D = ⟨X, U, f | R, s⟩where
X = Sn
i=1[xi −ϵ, xi + ϵ]; furthermore, for every ϵ-approximations ( ˆR, ˆs) of (R, s), the MinMax
Recurrent Unit ˆD = ⟨X, U, f | ˆR, ˆs⟩is strongly equivalent to D.
Proof. Let A = ⟨Q, Σ, δ⟩be an identity-reset semiautomaton with states Q = {q1, . . . , qn} and
inputs Σ = {σ1, . . . , σm}.
Construction.
We define the MinMax Recurrent Unit D = ⟨X, U, f | R, s⟩. Let U1, . . . , Um be
the pair-wise disjoint closed intervals such that U = Sm
i=1 Ui. Let xmax := max{x1, . . . , xn}, and
let xmin := min{x1, . . . , xn}.
Next we define the input functions R : U →{x1, . . . , xn} and s : U →{x1, . . . , xn}. For u ∈Ui,
letting τi(q) := δ(q, σi) be the state transformation induced by σi, we define
R(u) :=
xmin
if τi is identity,
xmax
if τi is constant,
s(u) :=
xmin
if τi is identity,
xj
if τi is constant with value qj.
Note that the functions R and s are continuous by Lemma 1 since they are constant on each η-
component Ui.
This completes the construction of the MinMax Recurrent Unit. Next we prove the claims.
η-finiteness.
The MinMax Recurrent Unit D is η-finite since U and X are finite unions of closed
intervals.
Realisation.
To show that D can realise A, it suffices to show that M(Cη(D)) is a realisation of
M(A). Note that M(A) = ⟨Q, Σ, δ, Γ, θ⟩where Γ = Q × Σ and θ = id.
For each i ∈[1..m], let us choose an arbitrary ui ∈Ui. The construction of D allows us to easily
derive that its canonical semiautomaton has the form Cη(D) = ⟨X, U, f⟩where
X = {x1, . . . , xn},
U = {u1, . . . , um} ,
f(x, u) = f(x, u),
∀x ∈{x1, . . . , xn}, ∀u ∈U.
Then M(Cη(D)) = ⟨X, U, f, X × U, id⟩.
To show that M(Cη(D)) is a realisation of M(A), it suffices to show an assignment (α, ι, ζ) of
M(Cη(A)) into M(A), satisfying the two conditions (I) and (II) below for every qi ∈Q and every
σj ∈Σ.

show (I), it suffices to show f(xi, uj) = xk.
We have that τ is a reset-identity transformation, and hence it is either identity or constant.
Say that τ is identity, hence qk = qi, and hence we need to show f(xi, uj) = xi. We have
f(xi, uj) = (xi ⊙xmax) ⊕xmin

= xi ⊕xmin

Now say that τ is constant with value qk, and hence it suffices to show f(xi, uj) = xk. We have
f(xi, uj) = (xi ⊙xmin) ⊕xk

= xmin ⊕xk

= xk
since xk ≤xmax by construction of xmax,
which proves the case when τ is constant.
This concludes the proof of (I), and hence the proof that the MinMax Recurrent Unit can realise A.
Strong equivalence of approximations.
Omitted since it is analogous to the one of Lemma 6.
The next lemma is the full version of Theorem 3 from Section 4.1, except that we postpone replacing
the input functions with MLPs.
Lemma 8. Let A be a permutation semiautomaton having n inputs and permutation degree d. Let
ϵ ≥0, let x1, . . . , xd ∈R with |xi −xj| > 2ϵ for all i ̸= j, and let U ⊆R be the union of n
pair-wise disjoint closed intervals. There exist continuous functions R : U →{x1, . . . , xd}d×d
and s : U →{x1, . . . , xd}d such that semiautomaton A can be realised by the η-finite MinMax
Recurrent Unit D = ⟨Xd, U, f | R, s⟩where X = Sd
i=1[xi −ϵ, xi + ϵ]; furthermore, for every
ϵ-approximations ( ˆR, ˆs) of (R, s), the MinMax Recurrent Unit ˆD = ⟨Xd, U, f | ˆR, ˆs⟩is strongly
equivalent to D.
Proof. Since A has permutation degree d, by Theorem 19, there exists φ : Σ →Sym([1..d])
such that the semiautomaton A′ = ⟨[1..d]d, Σ, δ′⟩with δ′(⟨q1, . . . , qd⟩, σ) = ⟨qπ(1), . . . , qπ(n)⟩for
π : [1..d] →[1..d] the permutation given by π = φ(σ). Let U1, . . . , Un be the pair-wise disjoint
closed intervals such that U = Sn
i=1 Ui. Let us enumerate the elements of Σ as σ1, . . . , σn. Let πi
be the permutation given by πi = φ(σi).
Construction.
We define the MinMax Recurrent Unit D = ⟨X, U, f | R, s⟩. Let xmax =
max{x1, . . . , xd}, let xmin = min{x1, . . . , xd}, and let x∆= xmax −xmin.
For each i ∈[1..n], let Pi ∈{0, 1}n×n be the (permutation) matrix where (Pi)j,k = 1 iff πi(k) = j,
and let ai : U →{0, x∆} be the function defined as ai(u) = x∆iff u ∈Ui. Then, we define the
functions R : U →{x1, . . . , xd}d×d and s : U →{x1, . . . , xd}d as
R(u) := xmin · 1d,d +
n
X
i=1
ai(u) · Pi
s(u) := xmin · 1d.
Note that each function ai is continuous since it is constant over η-components of U, and hence R is
continuous since it is a finite composition of continuous functions. Also s is continuous since it is
constant. This completes the construction of the MinMax Recurrent Unit. Next we prove the claims.
η-finiteness.
The MinMax Recurrent Unit D is η-finite since U is a finite union of closed intervals,
and X is the cross-product of a finite union of closed intervals.

D.3
Realising Cascades
This section develops part of the proof of Theorem 1. As discussed in the proof sketch of the theorem,
the expressivity results are obtained via realising cascades of semiautomata. In this section we show
that the MinMax Units obtained in the previous section can be composed into cascade to realise
cascades of semiautomata.
Theorem 20. Let us consider a cascade
C =

(i) one if Ai,j is identity-reset, (ii) the degree of Ai,j if Ai,j is a permutation semiautomaton, (iii) the
number of states of Ai,j otherwise.
Proof. We have that each Ai,j can be realised by an η-finite MinMax Recurrent Unit D′
i,j with MLPs
as input functions and state dimension as required. In fact, (i) when Ai,j is identity-reset, we can

The following theorem regards floating-point MinMax RNCs, that are defined in Section C.3.2.
Theorem 21. Let us consider a cascade
C =
 A1,1 ∥· · · ∥A1,m1


 Ad,1 ∥· · · ∥Ad,md


 D1,1 ∥· · · ∥D1,m1

⋉· · · ⋉
 Dd,1 ∥· · · ∥Dd,md

• if Ai,j is a permutation semiautomaton, then mi,j is the maximum between the degree and the
number of inputs of Ai,j;
• otherwise, mi,j is the maximum between the number of states and the number of inputs of Ai,j.

We have that each Ai,j can be realised by a MinMax Recurrent Unit D′
i,j over F with F-MLPs as

i,j

Theorem 22. Let us consider a cascade
C =
 A1,1 ∥· · · ∥A1,m1

 Ad,1 ∥· · · ∥Ad,md

 D1,1 ∥· · · ∥D1,m1


