Proof. It holds immediately by Theorem 20 and Theorem 21, which focus on the real-valued and
float-valued case, respectively.
D.4
Formal Expressivity (Proof of Theorem 1)
In this section we conclude the proof of Theorem 1 putting together the results obtained in the
previous sections. We prove it by parts, restating the theorem at the end and referring to all the proven
sub-theorems.
The next theorem corresponds to the first claim in Theorem 1.
Theorem 23. Every regular function F (regular language L) having degree d can be implemented
(recognised) by an η-finite MinMax RNC and by a floating-point MinMax RNC, both having state
degree d.
Proof. We first note that the case of regular languages is subsumed by the case of regular functions.
In fact, for every language L over an alphabet Σ, we have that a system can recognise L if it can
implement its characteristic function IL : Σ∗→{0, 1}, by definition, and such function is a regular
function. Thus, we proceed with the proof for functions.
Let F : Σ∗→Γ be a regular function having degree n. let AF be the canonical automaton of F
and let DF be the semiautomaton of AF . By Theorem 12, semiautomaton DF can be realised by a
cascade
C = A1 ⋉· · · ⋉An
where each Ai is either a two-state identity-reset semiautomaton or a G-semiautomaton for G a
simple group that is a factor of a subgroup of the transition monoid of DF . We note that, since F has
degree d by assumption, the permutation degree di of Ai is at most d.
The rest of the proof is split into two parts: in the first part we show the existence of an η-finite
MinMax RNC witnessing the claim, and in the second part we show the existence of a floating-point
MinMax RNC witnessing the claim-the arguments in the two parts are similar, but we keep the two
parts separate for the sake of clarity.
Part I.
By Theorem 22, we have that cascade C can be realised by a cascade
C′ = D1 ⋉· · · × Dn
where each Di is an η-finite MinMax Recurrent Unit with MLPs as input functions. Letting C′ =
⟨X, U, f⟩, by Theorem 15, we have that C′ can be extended into a system S = ⟨X, U, f, x0, X ×
U, id⟩that can implement F. In particular, system S has an identity output function, which can be
seen as a trivial MLP. Therefore, S is an η-finite MinMax RNC that can implement F, as required.
Part II.
By Theorem 21, we have that cascade C can be realised by a cascade
C′ = D1 ⋉· · · × Dn
where each Di is a floating-point MinMax Recurrent Unit over some floating-point domain F with
F-MLPs as input functions, both having state dimension equal to one if Ai is identity-reset and equal
to the degree di of Ai otherwise; we note that di ≤d. Letting C′ = ⟨X, U, f⟩, by Theorem 15, we
have that C′ can be extended into a system S = ⟨X, U, f, x0, X × U, id⟩that can implement F. In
particular, system S has an identity output function, which can be seen as a trivial F-MLP. Therefore,
S is a floating-point MinMax RNC that can implement F.
Next we show the following intermediate result in order to prove the second claim of Theorem 1.
Lemma 9. Let Σ and Γ be alphabets, and let S = ⟨X, U, f, x0, Y, g⟩be a MinMax Recurrent
Cascade. For every function F : Σ+ →Γ that is implemented by S with encoder enc : Σ →U and
decoder dec : Y →Γ, there exist finite subsets ˜X ⊆X, ˜U ⊆U, ˜Y ⊆Y such that the MinMax
Recurrent Cascade ˜S = ⟨˜X, ˜U, f, x0, ˜Y , g⟩implements F with the same encoder enc and decoder
dec.
Proof. Let us consider a finite input alphabet Σ and a finite output alphabet Γ.
Let S =
⟨X, U, f, x0, Y, g⟩be a MinMax RNC, and let F : Σ+ →Γ be a function that is implemented
by S with encoder enc : Σ →U and decoder dec : Y →Γ.
Since Σ is finite, the image of enc is a finite set ˜U := {u1, . . . , um} ⊆U.

We show there exists a finite ˜X with {x0} ⊆˜X ⊆X such that, for every input string σ1 · · · σℓ∈Σ+,

and that Y can be restricted to ˜Y := {g(x, u) ∈Y | x ∈˜X, u ∈˜U}.

The next theorem corresponds to the second claim of Theorem 1.
Theorem 24. Every function (language) that is not regular cannot be implemented (recognised) by a
MinMax Recurrent Cascade.
Proof. Let F be a function that can be implemented by a MinMax Recurrent Cascade S. By Lemma 9,
F can also be implemented by a MinMax Recurrent Cascade S′ that has a finite input and state
space, and hence trivially η-finite. It follows that F is regular by Theorem 13. The proof applies to
languages as well, since recognising a language means implementing its characteristic function.
We proceed towards proving the third claim of Theorem 1. We employ the notions of η-convergence
and aperiodicity from MAT, introduced in Section A.3.1.
Lemma 10. Let D = D1 ∥· · · ∥Dn be a parallel composition of η-finite aperiodic dynamics. Then
D is aperiodic.
Proof. We have that each Di is of the form Di = ⟨Xi, U, fi⟩. Then, we have that D is of the form
D = ⟨X1 × · · · × Xn, U, f⟩with f(⟨x1, . . . , xn⟩, u) = ⟨f1(x1, u), . . . , fn(xn, u)⟩.
Consider an input sequence u = (ut)t≥1 that is η-convergent in U. For each i ∈[1..n], let x0
i ∈Xi.
For each i ∈[1..n], we define the state sequence (xi, t)t≥1 with xi,t = Di(x0
i , u1 · · · ut) the state
of Dn on input u1 · · · ut when started from state x0
i ; and we define the state sequence (xt)t≥1
with xt = D(⟨x0
1, . . . , x0
n⟩, u1 · · · ut) the state of D on input u1 · · · ut when started from state
⟨x0
1, . . . , x0
n⟩.
By the form of the dynamics function of D noted above, we have that the state sequence (xt)t≥1 of
the given composition D equals the state sequence (⟨x1,t, . . . , xn,t⟩)t≥1.
Now, for every i ∈[1..n], we have that the sequence (xi,t)t≥1 is η-convergent since (ut)t≥1
is η-convergent by assumption, and Di is aperiodic by assumption.
Therefore, the sequence
(⟨x1,t, . . . , xn,t⟩)t≥1 is η-convergent, and hence the state sequence (xt)t≥1 is η-convergent, showing
that D is aperiodic.
Lemma 11. Let C = D1 ⋉· · · ⋉Dn be a cascade of η-finite aperiodic dynamics. Then C is
aperiodic.
Proof. We proceed by induction on n. In the base case n = 1 and the claim holds by assumption. In
the inductive case n > 1 and we assume by induction that the cascade Cn−1 = D1 ⋉· · · ⋉Dn−1 is
aperiodic. We have that Cn−1 and Dn are of the form
Cn−1 = ⟨Xn−1, U, fn−1⟩,
Dn = ⟨Xn, U × Xn−1, fn⟩.
Then, we have that C is of the form
C = ⟨Xn−1 × Xn, U, f⟩,
f(⟨xn−1, xn⟩, u) = ⟨fn−1(xn−1, u), fn(xn, ⟨u, xn−1⟩)⟩
Consider an input sequence u = (ut)t≥1 that is η-convergent in U. Let x0
n−1 ∈Xn−1 and
let x0
n ∈Xn. We define the state sequence (xn−1,t)t≥1 with xn−1,t = Cn−1(x0
n−1, u1 · · · ut)
the state of Cn−1 on input u1 · · · ut when started from state x0
n−1. Then we define the state se-
quence (xn,t)t≥1 with xn,t = Dn(x0
n, ⟨u1, xn−1,1⟩· · · ⟨ut, xn−1,t−1⟩) the state of Dn on input
⟨u1, xn−1,1⟩· · · ⟨ut, xn−1,t−1⟩when started from state x0
n. Finally, we define the state sequence
(xt)t≥1 with xt = C(⟨x0
n−1, x0
n⟩, u1 · · · ut) the state of C on input u1 · · · ut when started from state
⟨x0
n−1, x0
n⟩.
By the form of the dynamics function of C noted above, we have that the state sequence (xt)t≥1 of
the given cascade C equals the state sequence (⟨xn−1,t, xn,t⟩)t≥1.
Now, we have that the sequence (xn,t)t≥1 is η-convergent since (ut)t≥1 is η-convergent by as-
sumption, and Cn−1 is aperiodic by the inductive hypothesis. In turn, this implies that the state
sequence (xn,t)t≥1 is η-convergent, since Dn is aperiodic by assumption. Therefore, the sequence
(⟨xn−1,t, xn,t⟩)t≥1 is η-convergent, and hence the state sequence (xt)t≥1 is η-convergent, showing
that C is aperiodic.
Lemma 12. Every finite MinMax Recurrent Unit of state dimension one is aperiodic.
Proof. Let D = ⟨X, U, f | R, s⟩be a finite MinMax Recurrent Unit of state dimension one. Let
(ut)t≥1 be an η-convergent sequence in U. Since U is finite, its η-components are singleton, and
hence all inputs ut are the same input u ∈U. Let x ∈X, and let τ(x) := f(x, u). We have
τ(τ(x)) = f(f(x, u), u)

It follows that τ n = τ n+1 for all n ≥2.
Let us consider the state sequence (xt)t≥1 with xt = D(x, u1 · · · ut) the state of D on input u1 · · · ut
when started from state x. Since every ut equals u, we have that xt = τ t(x). Since τ t = τ t+1 for
all t ≥2, we have that xt = τ 2(x) for all t ≥2, and hence the sequence (xt)t≥1 is η-convergent,
showing that D is aperiodic.
The following theorem corresponds to the third claim of Theorem 1.
Theorem 25. Let S be a MinMax RNC of state degree one. Every regular function F that is not
group-free cannot be implemented by S.
Proof. Let F be a function implemented by a MinMax Recurrent Cascade of state degree one. By
Lemma 9, we have that F can be implemented by a MinMax Recurrent Cascade S having state degree
one, and a finite input and state space, and hence it is trivially η-finite. Then, by Lemma 12, we have
that each MinMax Recurrent Unit of S is aperiodic, and hence the dynamics of S are aperiodic by
Lemmas 10 and 11. By Theorem 16, it follows that Cη(S) is group-free. Then F is group-free since,
by Theorem 13 we have that Cη(S) can implement F. The proof applies to languages as well, since
recognising a language means implementing its characteristic function.
Theorem 1. Every regular function can be implemented by a MinMax RNC of state degree equal to
the degree of the function. Any function that is not regular cannot be implemented by a MinMax RNC.
Any function that is not group-free cannot be implemented by a MinMax RNC of state degree one.
Proof. The three claims of the theorem correspond to Theorems 23, 24, and 25 respectively.
Next we provide the floating-point counter-part of the theorem above.
Theorem 26. Every regular function can be implemented by a floating-point MinMax RNC of state
degree equal to the degree of the function. Any function that is not regular cannot be implemented
by a floating-point MinMax RNC. Any function that is not group-free cannot be implemented by a
floating-point MinMax RNC of state degree one.
Proof. The three claims of the theorem correspond to Theorems 23, 24, and 25 respectively.
Corollary 5. The functions that can be implemented by floating-point MinMax RNCs are the regular
functions, and the group-free functions if restricted to state degree one. The languages that can be
recognised by floating-point MinMax RNCs are the regular languages, and the star-free languages if
restricted to state degree one.
E
Algorithms
We present the algorithms to evaluate MinMax RNCs-their pseudocodes follow right below. For
sequential evaluation, we simply follows the recurrence. For parallel evaluation, we first note that
any two MinMax Recurrence operators, given by their matrix vector pairs ⟨A1, b1⟩, ⟨A2, b2⟩, can be
composed via the following associative operator:
⟨A1, b1⟩⊕⊗⟨A2, b2⟩:= ⟨A2 ⊗A1, (A2 ⊗b1) ⊕b2⟩.
Then we apply parallel scan [31, 40].
Thus, the algorithms in this appendix are mainly a basis for building rigorous proofs of the theorems
in Appendix F, where we show correctness and complexity of all the present algorithms, and overall
of MinMax Recurrence and MinMax RNCs.
Preliminary remarks on our pseudocode
We use common instructions with a notation that should be self-clear.
Parallel for.
To describe parallel algorithms, we use the parallel for instruction, cf. [31].
in parallel for i ←e1 to e2 do instructions
It corresponds to executing the block instructions in parallel providing to each thread a different
index i ranging from the value of expression e1 to the value of expression e2. Furthermore, we avoid
concurrent writes, thus satisfying the Concurrent Read Exclusive Write (CREW) constraint.
0-indexed tensors.
In this appendix and in Appendix F we adopt 0-indexed real-valued tensors.
We use shape(A) to obtain the shape of a tensor. We use A ∥B to concatenate tensors only when
they are vectors, making its meaning straightforward.
No side effects.
Although we adopt an imperative pseudocode with side effects, we avoid to assign
twice any variable or entry of a tensor. This makes the correctness proofs easier to develop.
E.1
Auxiliary Parallel Algorithms
In this subsection we provide the pseudocode of the auxiliary algorithms for parallel evaluation.
• Algorithm 1 is an immediate application of the parallel sum algorithm (cf. [31]) by replacing +
with ⊙, since the algorithm works for all associative operators, including ⊙.
• Algorithm 2 is analogous to the above Algorithm 2, except that now + is replaced with ⊕.
• Algorithm 3 is a straightforward computation entry-wise.
• Algorithm 4 is a straightforward algorithm for parallel matrix multiplication, with arithmetic
addition and multiplication replaced by {⊕, ⊙}.
• Algorithm 5 uses the above algorithms to evaluate the ⊕⊗operator, to compose a pair of MinMax
recurrences, used by parallel scan to compute multi-step compositions.
• Algorithm 6 applies the MinMax Recurrence operator to a given vector (specifically, the given
vector will be a state vector). It is used as the last step after calling parallel scan.
E.2
Sequential evaluation
We describe the sequential evaluation algorithm.
• SeqRecEval is the algorithm for sequential evaluation of MinMax Recurrence. It is a straightforward
algorithm that computes in order following the recurrence.
• SeqLayerEval is the algorithm for sequential evaluation of a MinMax Recurrent Layer. It evaluates
the neurons’ input functions by assuming a subroutine to evaluate MLPs, and by calling SeqRecEval
on the result of their their evaluation. Finally, it evaluates the layer’s output function, again by
calling the subroutine for MLPs.
• SeqCascEval is the algorithm for sequential evaluation of a MinMax RNC. It simply evaluates
layers iteratively by calling SeqRecEval.
E.3
Parallel Evaluation
We present the parallel evaluation a algorithm.
E.3.1
MinMax Parallel Scan
Parallel scan [39-41] is the core of the parallel evaluation algorithm.
Here we define the operator we use to compose MinMax Recurrence operators.
Definition 26. For A1, A2 ∈Rn×n and b1, b2 ∈Rn, the MinMax composition operator ⊕⊗is defined
as:
⟨A1, b1⟩⊕⊗⟨A2, b2⟩:= ⟨A2 ⊗A1, (A2 ⊗b1) ⊕b2⟩.
Then, parallel scan is used to evaluate all (exclusive) prefixes of an expression of the form:
⟨A1, b1⟩⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗⟨At, bt⟩.
(8)
The affine MinMax operator ⊕⊗is associative and it admits an identity element, which is the reason
why we can use parallel scan-formally proved in Appendix F.
First, parallel scan requires the expression in (8) to be a monoidal expression. Namely, the operator
⊕⊗must be associative, and there must be an identity element, which is1 an element ⟨E, e⟩such that
⟨E, e⟩⊕⊗⟨A, b⟩= ⟨A, b⟩⊕⊗⟨E, e⟩= ⟨A, b⟩
for all elements ⟨A, b⟩that can be generated by any intermediate composition
⟨A, b⟩= ⟨At1, bt1⟩⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗⟨At2, bt2⟩.
Algorithms.
We describe parallel scan and its subroutine for the identity element.
• Algorithm 7 generates the identity element ⟨E, e⟩out of two given values vmin and vmax, which in
the overall algorithm will be the largest and smallest scalars returned by unit’s input function on its
entire input sequence, also including the entries of the initial state. Intuitively, (vmin, vmax) will
mimick (−∞, +∞) which act as (0, 1) in the MinMax semiring (⊕, ⊙, −∞, +∞).2 Thus, E is
similar to the identity matrix In,n (replacing 1’s with +∞and 0’s with −∞) and e is similar to the
null vector 0n (replacing 0’s with −∞).
• Algorithm 8 follows the standard parallel scan algorithm (e.g., see [31]), combining pairs of
elements of the given expression, with some careful handling of the odd and even cases. It calls
Algorithm 7 in the case case, as it computes exclusive prefixes, hence identity on a given expression
of length 1.
E.3.2
Parallel Evaluation
Parallel evaluation of MinMax Recurrence, and then of layers and cascades, is straightforward once
parallel scan computes prefixes of recurrences.
• ParallelRecEval is the algorithm for parallel evaluation of MinMax Recurrence. It computes
vmin, vmax that are required by parallel scan. Then, it calls parallel scan (Algorithm 8) to compute
the sequence of matrices corresponding to prefixes of compositions of MinMax Recurrence. Then,
in parallel for every sequence step t, it includes the recurrence step at time t (since parallel scan
returns exclusive prefixes), and finally it applies the overall composition to the initial state, hence
obtaining the state at time t.
• ParLayerEval is the algorithm for parallel evaluation of a MinMax Recurrent Layer. It is as
SeqLayerEval, except that it handles each neuron in parallel, it calls ParallelRecEval, and then
computes the output’s layer in parallel wrt sequence steps.
• ParCascEval is the algorithm for parallel evaluation of a MinMax RNC. It is as ParCascEval, except
that it calls ParLayerEval to evaluate layers.
1allowing ourselves not to be entirely precise in this informal explanation for the sake for providing the intuition
2Potentially we could have used the MinMax semiring (⊕, ⊙, −∞, +∞) throughout the paper, instead of using only the
MinMax algebra (⊕, ⊙). However, we have preferred not to introduce infinities.
Algorithm 1: Minimum element of an array computed in parallel
Input
:Array A of length n ≥1
Output :Jn−1
i=0 A[i]
1 Function ParallelMin(A):
if n = 1 then
return A[0];
else
m ←⌊n/2⌋;
allocate array B of length m;
in parallel for i ∈[0..m −2] do
B[i] ←A[2i] ⊙A[2i + 1];
if n is even then
B[m −1] ←A[n −2] ⊙A[n −1];
else
B[m −1] ←A[n −3] ⊙A[n −2] ⊙A[n −1];
return ParallelMin(B);
Algorithm 2: Maximum element of an array computed in parallel
Input
:Array A of length n
Output :Ln−1
i=0 A[i]
1 Function ParallelMax(A):
if n = 1 then
return A[0];
else
m ←⌊n/2⌋;
allocate array B of length m;
in parallel for i ∈[0..m −2] do
B[i] ←A[2i] ⊕A[2i + 1];
if n is even then
B[m −1] ←A[n −2] ⊕A[n −1];
else
B[m −1] ←A[n −3] ⊕A[n −2] ⊕A[n −1];
return ParallelMax(B);
Algorithm 3: Parallel matrix addition
Input
:Matrices A, B ∈Rm×n
Output :Matrix C ∈Rm×n such that C = A ⊕B
1 Function ParallelAdd(A, B):
allocate matrix C of shape (m, n);
in parallel for i ←0 to m −1 do
in parallel for j ←0 to n −1 do
C[i, j] ←A[i, j] ⊕B[i, j];
return C;
Algorithm 4: Parallel matrix multiplication
Input
:Matrix A ∈Rm×p, matrix B ∈Rp×n
Output :Matrix C ∈Rm×n such that C = A ⊗B
1 Function ParallelMult(A, B):
allocate matrix C of shape (m, n);
allocate tensor T of shape (m, n, p);
in parallel for i ←0 to m −1 do
in parallel for j ←0 to n −1 do
in parallel for k ←0 to p −1 do
T [i, j, k] ←A[i, k] ⊙B[k, j];
C[i, j] ←ParallelMax(T [i, j]);
return C;
Algorithm 5: Parallel MinMax Composition
Input
:(A1, B1) ∈Rn×n × Rn, (A2, B2) ∈Rn×n × Rn
Output :(A, B) ∈Rn×n × Rn such that ⟨A, B⟩= ⟨A1, B1⟩⊕⊗⟨A2, B2⟩
1 Function ParallelComp(A1, B1, A2, B2):
allocate tensor A of shape (n, n);
allocate arrays B, Z of length n;
A ←ParallelMult(A2, A1);
Z ←ParallelMult(A2, B1);
B ←ParallelAdd(Z, B2);
return (A, B);
Algorithm 6: Parallel MinMax Operator Application
Input
:tensor A of shape (N, N), array B of length N, array X of length N
Output :tensor Y such that Y = (A ⊗X) ⊕B
1 Function ParallelApply(A, B, X):
allocate arrays Z, Y of length N;
Z ←ParallelMult(A, X);
Y ←ParallelAdd(Z, B);
return Y ;
Algorithm 7: Compute identity element in parallel
Input
:scalars vmin, vmax ∈R and n ∈N>0
Output :identity (E, e) for MinMax monoid M[vmin,vmax],n
1 Function ParallelIdentity(vmin, vmax, n):
allocate tensor E of shape (N, N);
in parallel for i ∈[0..N −1) do
in parallel for j ∈[0..N) do
if i = j then E[i, j] ←vmax ;
else E[i, j] ←vmin ;
allocate array e of length N;
in parallel for i ∈[0..N) do
e[i] ←vmin;
return (E, e);
Algorithm 8: Parallel Scan
Input
:tensor A of shape (T, N, N), tensor B of shape (T, N), scalars vmin, vmax;
Output :tensors (C, D) with (C[t], D[t]) = (A[0], B[0]) ⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗(A[t −1], B[t −1]) ⊕⊗E for all
t ∈[0..T), with E the identity of the MinMax monoid M[vmin,vmax],N
1 Function ParallelScan(A, B, vmin, vmax):
(T, N) ←shape(B);
if T = 1 then
return ParallelIdentity(vmin, vmax, N);
m ←⌊T/2⌋; ℓ←⌈T/2⌉;
allocate tensor X of shape (ℓ, N, N);
allocate tensor Y of shape (ℓ, N);
in parallel for t ∈[0..m −1] do
(X[t], Y [t]) ←ParallelComp(A[2t], B[2t], A[2t + 1], B[2t + 1]);
if T is odd then
(X[ℓ−1], Y [ℓ−1]) ←(A[T −1], B[T −1]);
(Z, W) ←ParallelScan(X, Y, vmin, vmax);
allocate tensor C of shape (T, N, N);
allocate tensor D of shape (T, N);
in parallel for t ∈[0..T −1] do
if t is even then
(C[t], D[t]) ←(Z[t/2], W [t/2]);
else
(C[t], D[t]) ←ParallelComp(Z[(t −1)/2], W [(t −1)/2], A[t −1], B[t −1]);
return (C, D);
Function SeqRecEval(Xinit, A, B)
Input
:tensors ⟨Xinit, A, B⟩with Xinit ∈RN, A ∈RT ×N×N, B ∈RT ×N;
Output :tensor X with X[0] = MinMaxRec(Xinit, A[0], B[0]) and
X[t] = MinMaxRec(X[t −1], A[t], B[t]) for all t ∈[1..T);
1 (T, N) ←shape(B);
2 allocate tensor X of shape (T, N);
3 allocate tensor Z of shape (T + 1, N + 1, N);
4 Z[0, N] ←Xinit;
5 for t ←1 to T do
for i ←1 to N do
Z[t, 0, i −1] ←B[t −1, i −1];
for j ←1 to N do
Z[t, j, i −1] ←Z[t, j −1, i −1] ⊕
 A[t −1, i −1, j −1] ⊙Z[t −1, N, j −1]
;
X[t −1] ←Z[t, N];
11 return X;
Function SeqLayerEval(W, U)
Input
:(W, U) where W are the weights of a MinMax Layer S and U ∈RT ×din for din the
input dimension of S;
Output :Y ∈RT ×dout for dout the output dimension of S, such that Y = S(U);
1 (T, din) ←shape(U); (nunits, dstate) ←shape(Xinit); (dout, _) ←shape(W out);
2 (W R, W s, W out, Xinit) ←W;
3 allocate tensor X of shape (T + 1, nunits · dstate);
4 for i ←0 to nunits −1 do
allocate tensor Ai of shape (T, dstate, dstate);
allocate tensor Bi of shape (T, dstate);
for t ←0 to T −1 do
Ai[t] ←EvaluateMLP(W R[i], U[t]);
Bi[t] ←EvaluateMLP(W s[i], U[t]);
pi ←i · dstate;
qi ←(i + 1) · dstate;
X[0, pi : qi] ←Xinit[i];
X[1 : , pi : qi] ←SeqRecEval(Xinit[i], Ai, Bi);
14 allocate tensor Y of shape (T, dout);
15 for t ←0 to T −1 do
Y [t] ←EvaluateMLP(W out, X[t] ∥X[t + 1] ∥U[t]);
17 return Y ;
Function SeqCascEval(W, U)
Input
:⟨W, U⟩where W are the weights of an RNC S, and U ∈RT ×din for din the input
dimension of S;
Output :Y ∈RT ×dout for dout the output dimension of S, such that Y = S(U);
1 nlayers ←length(W);
2 Y ←U;
3 for i ←0 to nlayers −1 do
Y ←SeqLayerEval(W [i], Y );
5 return Y ;
Function ParallelRecEval(Xinit, A, B)
Input
:array Xinit of length N, tensor A of shape (T, N, N), tensor B of shape (T, N);
Output :tensor X of shape (T, N) such that X[0] = MinMaxRec(Xinit, A[0], B[0]) and
X[t] = MinMaxRec(X[t −1], A[t], B[t]) for every t ∈(0..T);
1 (T, N) ←shape(B);
2 vmin ←ParallelMin(Xinit ∥flat(A) ∥flat(B));
3 vmax ←ParallelMax(Xinit ∥flat(A) ∥flat(B));
4 (C, D) ←ParallelScan(A, B, vmin, vmax);
5 allocate tensors X, W of shape (T, N);
6 allocate tensor Z of shape (T, N, N);
7 in parallel for t ←0 to T −1 do
(Z[t], W [t]) ←ParallelComp(C[t], D[t], A[t], B[t]);
X[t] ←ParallelApply(Z[t], W [t], Xinit);
10 return X;
Function ParLayerEval(W, U)
Input
:(W, U) where W are the weights of a MinMax Layer S and U ∈RT ×din for din the
input dimension of S;
Output :Y such that Y = S(U);
1 (T, din) ←shape(U); (nunits, dstate) ←shape(Xinit); (dout, _) ←shape(W out);
2 (W R, W s, W out, Xinit) ←W;
3 allocate tensor X of shape (T + 1, nunits · dstate);
4 in parallel for i ←0 to nunits −1 do
allocate tensor Ai of shape (T, dstate, dstate);
allocate tensor Bi of shape (T, dstate);
in parallel for t ←0 to T −1 do
Ai[t] ←EvaluateMLP(W R[i], U[t]);
Bi[t] ←EvaluateMLP(W s[i], U[t]);
pi ←i · dstate;
qi ←(i + 1) · dstate;
X[0, pi : qi] ←Xinit[i];
X[1 : , pi : qi] ←ParallelRecEval(Xinit[i], Ai, Bi);
14 allocate tensor Y of shape (T, dout);
15 in parallel for t ←0 to T −1 do
Y [t] ←EvaluateMLP(W out, X[t] ∥X[t + 1] ∥U[t]);
17 return Y ;
Function ParCascEval(W, U)
Input
:⟨W, U⟩where W are the weights of an RNC S, and U ∈RT ×din for din the input
dimension of S;
Output :Y ∈RT ×dout for dout the output dimension of S, such that Y = S(U);
1 nlayers ←length(W);
2 Y ←U;
3 for i ←0 to nlayers −1 do
Y ←ParLayerEval(W [i], Y );
5 return Y ;
F
Complexity Proofs
In this Appendix we prove the theorems of Section 5. We first formally define the relevant computation
problems, then we prove the sequential complexity results, and finally the parallel complexity results.
F.1
Definition of the Relevant Computation Problems
We define the relevant compuation problems, formalising the tasks for which we want to show their
complexity.
Definition 27 (MinMax Recurrence Evaluation). MinMax Recurrence Evaluation is the computation
problem where inputs and outputs are respectively given by
I :=
∞
[
T =1
∞
[
N=1
IT,N
with IT,N :=

Definition 28 (Representation of MinMax RNCs). The size of a linear function is s = ⟨din, dout⟩.
The weights for a linear function of size s are
Wlin
s
:= {⟨A, b⟩| A ∈Rdout×din, b ∈Rdout}
The linear function having weights W = ⟨A, b⟩∈Wlinear
s
is the function f : Rdin →Rdout given by
f(x) = Ax + b,
and denoted by Lin(W) := f.
The size of an MLP is s = ⟨din, dout, dh, nh⟩with input dimension din, output dimension dout,
hidden dimension dh, and number of hidden layers nh. The weights for an MLP of size s are
Wmlp
s
:= {⟨W in, ⟨W h
0 , . . . , W h
nh−1⟩, W out⟩| W in ∈Wlin
din,dh, W h
i ∈Wlin
dh,dh, W out ∈Wlin
dh,dout}
The MLP having weights W = ⟨W in, W h, W out⟩∈Wmlp
s
is the function Rdin →Rdout given by
MLP(W) := Lin(W in) ◦ϕ ◦Lin(W h[0]) ◦ϕ ◦· · · ◦Lin(W h[nh −1]) ◦ϕ ◦Lin(W out),
where ϕ : R →R is the activation function applied element-wise.
The size of a MinMax Neural Layer is s = ⟨din, dmlp, nmlp, dout, nunits, dstate⟩with input dimension
din, hidden dimension of MLPs dmlp, number of hidden layers of MLPs nmlp, output dimension
dout, number of units nunits, and state dimension of each unit dstate.
The weights of the input functions of a MinMax Neural Layer of size s are
Wlayer,R

:= {⟨W R

0, . . . , W s
nunits−1⟩| W s
i ∈Wmlp
ss }
with ss = ⟨din, dstate, ⌈dmlp/nunits⌉, nmlp⟩,
and the weights of a MinMax Neural Layer of size s are
Wlayer
s
:= {⟨W R, W s, W out, Xinit⟩| W R ∈Wlayer,R

The MinMax Layer Layer(W) with weights W = ⟨W R, W s, W out, Xinit⟩∈Wlayer
s
is the MinMax
Layer where the output function is MLP(W out), the reset function of unit i is MLP(W R
i ), and the
set function of unit i is MLP(W R
i ).
The set of possible sizes of a MinMax RNC is Srnc := (N>0)8-the set of 8-tuples of non-zero
natural numbers. A size s = ⟨din, dout, dmodel, dstate, nunits, nlayers, dmlp, nmlp⟩∈Srnc is to be
interpreted as follows:
• din is the input dimension, hence the input dimension of the first layer;
• dout is the output dimension, hence the output dimension of the last layer;
• dmodel is the dimension of the output of each layer except the last one, and also the input of all
layers except the first one;
• dstate is the state dimension of each MinMax Recurrent Unit, hence the state degree of the RNC;
• nunits is the number of MinMax Recurrent Units in each layer;
• nlayers is the number of MinMax Layers;
• dmlp is the dimension of the hidden layers of each MLP;
• nmlp is the number of hidden layers of each MLP.
The weights of a MinMax RNC of size s are
Wrnc
s
:= {⟨W0, . . . , Wnlayers−1⟩| Wi ∈Wlayer
si
},
where
s0 = ⟨din, dmodel, dstate, nunits, dmlp, nmlp⟩,
si = ⟨dmodel, dmodel, dstate, nunits, dmlp, nmlp⟩,
∀i ∈[1..nlayers −2],
snlayers−1 = ⟨dmodel, dout, dstate, nunits, dmlp, nmlp⟩.
The MinMax RNC having weights W = ⟨W0, . . . , Wnlayers−1⟩∈Wrnc
s
, denoted by RNC(W), is
the MinMax RNC S0 ⋉· · · ⋉Snlayers−1 where Si = Layer(Wi).

F.2
Complexity of Sequential Evaluation
In this section we prove the theorems of Section 5 regarding sequential evaluation. The sequential
complexity claimed in Theorem 4 is given by Lemma 13. Then, Theorem 5 is given by its more
formal variant Theorem 27.
Lemma 13. SeqRecEval solves MinMax Recurrence Evaluation in time O(TN 2) for T the number
of steps and N the state dimension.
Proof. Let ⟨Xinit, A, B⟩be an input to the problem.
Correctness.
We show that SeqRecEval solves the problem.
To distinguish variables of the algorithm from mathematical variables, let us add a hat to the
variables of the algorithm. In particular, the input variables are ⟨ˆXinit, ˆA, ˆB⟩, and the other variables
are ˆT, ˆN, ˆX, ˆZ (along with the loop indices). We note that the input variables ⟨ˆXinit, ˆA, ˆB⟩are
never modified by the algorithm, and hence we can write ˆXinit, ˆA, ˆB to denote their values, with no
ambiguity. Furthermore, the scalar variables ˆT, ˆN as well as the entries of tensors ˆX and ˆZ are written
at most once and never read before they are written, and hence we can write ˆT, ˆN, ˆX[t, i], ˆZ[t, i, j] to
denote the values held by the variables, with no ambiguity when such values have been assigned.
Let us define X as the tensor of shape (T + 1, N, N) satisfying X[0] = Xinit and X[t] =
MinMaxRec(X[t −1], A[t −1], B[t −1]) for every t ∈[1..T].
It suffices to show that ˆX[0 : T −1] = X[1 : T] when Line 11 is executed.
We have that the value of the input variables ⟨ˆXinit, ˆA, ˆB⟩is ⟨Xinit, A, B⟩. Then, after executing
Line 1, the value of ˆT is T and the value of ˆN is N.
We first show by induction on t from 0 to T that after having executed Line 1-4 and after t iterations
of the loop of Line 5 the equalities ˆZ[0 : t, N] = X[0 : t] and ˆX[0 : t −1] = X[1 : t] hold. The base
case t = 0 holds since, after having executed Line 1-4 and before executing any iteration of the loop,
the equality ˆZ[0, N] = X[0] = Xinit holds by Line 4, and the equality ˆX[0 : −1] = X[1 : 0] holds
vacously. In the inductive case we have t > 0, hence the loop has been executed t times, and we
assume by induction that the equalities ˆZ[0 : t −1, N] = X[0 : t −1] and ˆX[0 : t −2] = X[1 : t −1]
hold. Letting i ∈[1..N], we show that that the i-th iteration of the loop of Line 6 ensures that
the equality ˆZ[t, N, 0 : i −1] = X[t, 0 : i −1] holds. We have that right after executing Line 7,
ˆZ[t, 0, i −1] = B[t −1, i −1]. We proceed by a second induction on j from 0 to N, showing that that
after j iterations of the loop of Line 8, the following equality holds:
ˆZ[t, j, i −1] = B[t −1, i −1] ⊕
j−1
M
ℓ=0
 A[t −1, i −1, ℓ] ⊙X[t −1, ℓ]
.
In the base case we have j = 0, and the equality holds by the assignment of Line 7. The inductive
case j > 0 holds since
ˆZ[t, j, i −1]
= ˆZ[t, j −1, i −1] ⊕
 A[t −1, i −1, j −1] ⊙ˆZ[t −1, N, j −1]

Hence, by Line 10, we have ˆX[t −1] = ˆZ[t, N] = X[t], which proves the induction on t. Therefore,
ˆX[t −1] = X[t] for every t ∈[1..T], as required.
Execution time.
Since reading and writing a real number, as well as executing an elementary
operation on real numbers, take constant time in the assumed model of computation, we have that the
execution time is O(ℓ) for ℓthe total number of times Line 9 is executed, and hence it is O(TN 2) as
claimed.
Lemma 14. On every input ⟨W, U⟩for W ∈Wlayer
s
of size s = ⟨din, dmlp, nmlp, dout, nunits, dstate⟩,
and U ∈RT ×din, the output of SeqLayerEval is S(U) for S = Layer(W), and its execution time is
O

T ·
 nunits · d2

Proof. We first show correctness and then complexity. We have that the dynamics D of S are given
by a parallel composition D1 ∥· · · ∥Dn with each Di a MinMax Recurrent Unit.
Correctness.
After Line 1, the values of T, din, nunits, dstate, dout are correctly set according to
the shape of U and the definition of W ∈Wlayer
s
(Definition 28). After Line 2, the values of
W R, W s, W out, Xinit are correctly set according to the definition of W ∈Wlayer
s
(Definition 28).
Then the algorithm proceeds to computing the states of the MinMax Layer S on the given input U.
Each MinMax Unit of the layer has state dimension dstate, and hence the tensor allocated in Line 3
suffices to store the initial state and the subsequent states of all units. Let us now consider an iteration
of the loop of Lines 4-13 for index i ∈[0..nunits −1]. Each iteration is independent from the others,
as it does not read values written in other iterations, nor assigns a value to a memory location that is
also written in some other iteration. After executing Lines 5-9, we have that, for every t ∈[0..T −1],
the value of Ai[t] is Ri(U[t]) for Ri = MLP(W R[i]) the reset function of component i of the layer,
and the value of Bi[t] is si(U[t]) for si = MLP(W s[i]) the set function of component i of the layer.
After executing Lines 10-12, we have that X[0, i · dstate : (i + 1) · dstate] = Xinit[i], and after executing
Line 13, by Theorem 13 we have that X[t + 1, i · dstate : (i + 1) · dstate] = Di(Xinit[i], U[0 : t]) for
every t ∈[0..T −1]. Thus, after the execution of the loop of Lines 4-13 completes, we have that
X[t + 1] = D(Xinit, U[0 : t]) for every t ∈[0..T −1].
Next, let us consider an iteration of the loop of Lines 15-16 for t ∈[0..T −1], noting that each
iteration is independent from the others. We have that
X[t] ∥X[t + 1] ∥U[t] = ⟨D(Xinit, U[0 : t −1]), D(Xinit, U[0 : t]), U[t]⟩,
and hence after executing Line 16, we have
Y [t] = h
 D(Xinit, U[0 : t −1]), D(Xinit, U[0 : t]), U[t]
for h = MLP(W out) the output function of S. Thus, after the execution of the loop of Lines 15-16
completes, we have that Y = S(U) as required.

F.2.1
Correctness and Complexity of The Auxiliary Parallel Algorithms

The work is given by

O(1)
if n = 1,
work(⌊n/2⌋) + O(n)
otherwise.
Therefore, depth(n) = O(log n) and work(n) = O(n) by the Master Theorem, cf. [54].
Theorem 29. On any given input A ∈Rn, the output of ParallelMax (Algorithm 2) is Ln−1
i=0 A[i],
its depth is O(log n), and its work is O(n).
Proof. Omitted as it is analogous to ParallelMin (Theorem 28).
Theorem 30. On any given input A, B ∈Rm×n, the output of ParallelAdd (Algorithm 3) is
C = A ⊕B, its depth is O(1), and its work is O(m · n).
Proof. Correctness is immediate as the algorithm closely follows the definition of A ⊕B. Depth is
O(1) since the algorithm allocates C (Line 2) which has constant cost, and then it executes Line 5 in
parallel of each value of i, j. Work is O(m · n) since the algorithm allocates C (Line 2) which has
constant cost, and then it executes Line 5 for each value of i ∈[0..m −1] and j ∈[0..n −1], and
hence it executes Line 5 m · n times overall.
Theorem 31. On any given input A ∈Rm×p, B ∈Rp×n, the output of ParallelMult (Algorithm 4)
is C = A ⊗B, its depth is O(log p), and its work is O(m · n · p).
Proof. We first show correctness and the complexity.
Correctness.
We have that, for each i ∈[0..m −1], each j ∈[0..n −1], and each k ∈[0..p −1],
after executing Line 7, it holds that
T [i, j, k] = A[i, k] ⊙B[k, j].
Thus, by Theorem 29, for each i ∈[0..m −1] and each j ∈[0..n −1], after executing Line 8, it
holds that
C[i, j] =
p−1
M
k=0
T [i, j, k] =
p−1
M
k=0
(A[i, k] ⊙B[k, j]),
which is the required value for C[i, j].
Complexity.
The depth is O(1) plus the maximum depth of ParallelMax on an input T [i, j]. Since
each T [i, j] has length p, by Theorem 29 we have that the depth of each call of ParallelMax has
depth O(log p). Thus, the algorithm has depth O(log p) overall.
The work is O((m · n) · (c + w)) for c the cost of Line 7 (which is executed m · n times) and for
w the maximum work of a call of ParallelMax on input T [i, j]. Since each T [i, j] has length p, by
Theorem 29 we have that the work of each call of ParallelMax is O(p). Thus, since c is constant,
the algorithm has work O(m · n · p) overall.
Theorem 32. On any given input (A1, B1) ∈Rn×n × Rn, (A2, B2) ∈Rn×n × Rn, the output of
ParallelComp (Algorithm 5) is ⟨A, B⟩= ⟨A1, B1⟩⊕⊗⟨A2, B2⟩, its depth is O(log n), and its work
is O(n3).
Proof. We first show correctness and the complexity.
Correctness.
By Theorem 31, after executing Line 4 it holds that A = A2 ⊗A1, and after executing
Line 5 it holds that Z = A2 ⊗B1. Then, by Theorem 30, after executing Line 6 it holds that
B = Z ⊕B2 = (A2 ⊗B1) ⊕B2. Thus, the returned pair (A, B) is as required.
Complexity.
The depth is O(d1 + d2 + d3) for d1 the depth of ParallelMult(A2, A1), d2 the
depth of ParallelMult(A2, B1), and d3 the depth of ParallelAdd(Z, B2). By Theorem 31, we
have that d1 and d2 are O(log n). By Theorem 30, we have that d3 is O(1). Therefore, the algorithm
has depth O(log n + log n + 1) = O(log n).
The work is O(w1 + w2 + w3) for w1 the work of ParallelMult(A2, A1), w2 the work of
ParallelMult(A2, B1), and w3 the work of ParallelAdd(Z, B2). By Theorem 31, we have
that w1 is O(n3) and w2 is O(n2). By Theorem 30, we have that w3 is O(n). Therefore, the
algorithm has work O(n3 + n2 + n) = O(n3).
Theorem 33. On any given input (A, B, X) ∈Rn×n × Rn × Rn, the output of ParallelApply
(Algorithm 6) is C = (A ⊗X) ⊕B, its depth is O(log n), and its work is O(n2).
Proof. We first show correctness. After Line 2 we have that Z = A ⊗X by Theorem 31. Hence by
Theorem 30, after Line 3 Y = Z ⊕B = (A ⊗X)⊕as required.
Next we analyse its complexity. The depth is O(log n) since it is the sum of the following depths:
• the depth of ParallelMult on input (A, X), which is O(log n) by Theorem 31;
• the depth of ParallelAdd on input (Z, B), which is O(1) by Theorem 30.
The work is O(n2) since it is the sum of the following work:
• the work of ParallelMult on input (A, X), which is O(n2) by Theorem 31;
• the work of ParallelAdd on input (Z, B), which is O(n) by Theorem 30.
F.2.2
Parallel Complexity of Evaluation
The claim for parallel complexity in Theorem 4 is given by Lemma 16. Then,Theorem 6 is given by
its more formal and general variant Theorem 37, which also show the complexity without collapsing
any quantity describing the size of the RNC, also without fixing the number of hidden layers of
MLPs.
Definition 26. For A1, A2 ∈Rn×n and b1, b2 ∈Rn, the MinMax composition operator ⊕⊗is defined
as:
⟨A1, b1⟩⊕⊗⟨A2, b2⟩:= ⟨A2 ⊗A1, (A2 ⊗b1) ⊕b2⟩.
Definition 30. For n ∈N>0 and for V a compact subset V ⊂R, we define the MinMax monoid
over V of dimension n as MV,n := ⟨V n×n × V n, ⊕⊗⟩. We drop the subscripts V and n, writing M
instead of MV,n, when specifying them is not relevant or they are clear from the context.

Closedness.

Then C = A2 ⊗A1 ∈V n×n since the operator ⊗yields an n-by-n matrix C when applied to a pair
of n-by-n matrices A2, A1, and the elements of C are elements occurring in A2, A1 (hence in V )
since they are obtained via min and max operations.

Definition 31. Let n ∈N>0, let V be a compact subset V ⊂R, let MV,n = ⟨V n×n × V n, ⊕⊗⟩be

Theorem 34. On input (vmin, vmax, N), the output of ParallelIdentity (Algorithm 7) is the
identity element E of the MinMax monoid M[vmin,vmax],N, its depth is O(1) and its work is O(N 2).
Proof. Correctness is immediate as the algorithm closely follows the characterisation of E as given
in Proposition 3. The depth is O(1) as the algorithm consists of two subsequent parallel blocks, at
Lines 3-6 and Lines 7-9, respectively; and each parallel block consists of O(1) instructions executed
in parallel. The work is O(N 2) as the algorithm performs work O(N 2) to assign values to the
matrix E of shape (N, N) (Lines 3-6) and work O(N) to assign values to the array e of length N
(Lines 7-9).
Theorem 35. Let vmin, vmax ∈R with vmin ≤vmax, let V = [vmin, vmax], let A ∈V T ×N×N, and
let B ∈V T ×N. On input ⟨A, B, vmin, vmax⟩, the output of ParallelScan (Algorithm 8) is (C, D)
with

and its work is O(TN 3).

Correctness.

| As E is identity and t > 0

as required. This completes the correctness proof.
Complexity.
We first analyse the depth. When T = 1, the depth is given by the depth of
ParallelIdentity(vmin, vmax, N) which is O(1) by Theorem 34. When T > 1, the depth is
asymptotically bounded by the depth of the recursive call at Line 12 on inputs of length ⌈T/2⌉and
dimension N, plus the depth of ParallelComp on inputs of dimension N, which is O(log N) by
Theorem 32. Thus, there exist constants a1, c, c1 such that the depth is bounded as follows:

(

Lemma 16. ParallelRecEval solves MinMax Recurrence Evaluation with depth O(log N · log T)
and work O(TN 3) for T the number of steps and N the state dimension. Thus, it runs in time
O(log N · log T + TN 3/P) given P processors.
Proof. Let (Xinit, A, B) be an input of MinMax Recurrence Evaluation. We first show correctness
and then complexity.
Correctness.
Let Y ∈RT ×N be such that Y [0] = MinMaxRec(Xinit, A[0], B[0]) and Y [t] =
MinMaxRec(Y [t −1], A[t], B[t]) for every t ∈[1..T −1]. It suffices to show that the variable X of
the algorithm satisfies X = Y when the return instruction of Line 10 is executed.
After executing Line 2, we have that vmin is the minimum scalar value occurring in the input, by
Theorem 28. After executing Line 3, we have that vmax is the minimum scalar value occurring in the
input, by Theorem 29. After executing Line 4, since each ⟨A[t], B[t]⟩belonds to the MinMax monoid
M[vmin,vmax,N, by Theorem 35 we have that (C, D) is such that
(C[t], D[t]) = (A[0], B[0]) ⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗(A[t −1], B[t −1]) ⊕⊗E,
∀t ∈[0..T −1],
(44)
for E the identity element of the MinMax monoid M[vmin,vmax,N. Let us now consider Lines 7-9 for
t ∈[0..T −1]. After executing Line 8, it holds that
(Z[t], W [t])
(45)
= (C[t], D[t]) ⊕⊗(A[t], B[t])
| Line 8 and Theorem 32
(46)
=
 (A[0], B[0]) ⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗(A[t −1], B[t −1]) ⊕⊗E

⊕⊗(A[t], B[t])
| Eq. (44)
(47)
= (A[0], B[0]) ⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗(A[t −1], B[t −1]) ⊕⊗E ⊕⊗(A[t], B[t])
| associativity
(48)
= (A[0], B[0]) ⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗(A[t −1], B[t −1]) ⊕⊗(A[t], B[t])
| E is identity
(49)
= (A[0], B[0]) ⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗(A[t], B[t]).
(50)
After executing Line 9, by Theorem 33 it holds that
X[t] = (Z[t] ⊗Xinit) ⊕W [t].
(51)
We conclude the proof by showing that the r.h.s. above equals Y [t] as required. Let us recall the
default action τ of the monoid M[vmin,vmax],N (Definition 31), and let us introduce the notation
τi(x) := τ(A[i], B[i], x) for each i ∈[0..T −1], and then the notation τ0:i := (τ0 ◦· · · ◦τi) for each
i ∈[0..T −1]. By the definition of the default action, we have
Y [t] = τ0:t(Xinit),
(52)
and by Proposition 4 we have
τ0:t = τMt
for Mt := (A[0], B[0]) ⊕⊗· · · ⊕⊗(A[t], B[t]).
(53)
Then,
Y [t] = τ0:t(Xinit)
| Eq. (52)
(54)
= τMt(Xinit)
| Eq. (53)
(55)
= τ(Z[t], W[t], Xinit)
| Mt = (Z[t], W [t]) by Eq. (50)
(56)
= (Z[t] ⊗Xinit) ⊕W [t]
| def. of τ
(57)
= X[t],
| Eq. (51)
(58)
as required.
Complexity.
The depth is given by the sum of:
• the sum of the depths of ParallelMin and ParallelMax (Lines 2-3) on input of length N +
TN 2 + TN, each having depth O(log(N + TN 2 + TN)) by Theorems 28,29, and hence the sum
of their depths is 2 · O(log(N + TN 2 + TN)) = O(log(TN)) = O(log T + log N);
• the depth of ParallelScan (Line 4) on an input having T steps and dimension N, which is
O(log T · log N) by Theorem 35;
• the maximum for t ∈[0..T −1] of
• the depth of ParallelComp (Line 8) on input of dimension N, which is O(log N) by Theorem 32;
• the depth of ParallelApply (Line 9) on input of dimension N, which is O(log N) by Theo-
rem 33.
Thus the depth is O(log T + log N) + O(log T · log N) + O(log N), which is O(log T · log N) as
claimed.
The work is given by the sum of:
• the sum of the work of ParallelMin and ParallelMax (Lines 2-3) on input of length N +TN 2+
TN, each performing work O(N + TN 2 + TN) by Theorems 28,29, and hence their total work is
2 · O(N + TN 2 + TN) = O(TN 2);
• the work of ParallelScan (Line 4) on an input having T steps and dimension N, which is
O(TN 3) by Theorem 35;
• the sum of the work of the following for t ∈[0..T −1]:
• the work of ParallelComp (Line 8) on input of dimension N, which is O(N 3) by Theorem 32;
• the work of ParallelApply (Line 9) on input of dimension N, which is O(N 2) by Theorem 33.
Thus the work is O(TN 2) + O(TN 3) + T · (O(N 3) + O(N 2)), which is O(TN 3) as claimed.
Theorem 36. MinMax Recurrence Evaluation can be solved in parallel with depth O(log N · log T)
and work O(TN 3) for T the number of steps and N the state dimension. Thus, it can be solved in
time O(log N ·log T +TN 3/P) given P processors, and hence in O(log N ·log T) if P ∈Ω(TN 3).
Proof. Immediate by Lemma 16.

Lemma 17; and the loops in SeqCascEval have independent iterations and hence their iterations can
be executed in parallel.
Then, the depth and work are given by the sum of depth and work, respectively, of the calls to

⟨dstate, nunits, dmlp, nmlp⟩. By Lemma 17 the depth of the execution for the first layer is

G
Stability Proofs
In this section we prove Theorem 7. First we provide general definitions of stability.
G.1
BIBS and BIBO Stability
In order to state the results in a more rigorous manner, we define the notion of uniformly bounded-input
bounded-state (BIBS) stability for dynamics and system, and the notion of uniformly bounded-input
bounded-output (BIBO) stability for systems.
Definition 32. Parametric dynamics D, having state space X, input space U, and parameter space Θ,
are uniformly bounded-input bounded-state (BIBS) stable when started in state x0 ∈X if, for every
constant B ∈R, there exists a constant C ∈R such that, for every parameter value θ ∈Θ and every
input sequence u1 · · · uT ∈U +,
∥θ∥≤B ∧
max
t∈[1..T ] ∥ut∥≤B
=⇒
max
t∈[1..T ] ∥xt∥≤C,
where xt = D(x0, u1 · · · ut; θ) is the state at time t ∈[1..T]. A parametric system S is uniformly
BIBS if its dynamics are so when started in the initial state of S.
Definition 33. A parametric system S, having input space U and parameter space Θ, is uniformly
bounded-input bounded-output (BIBO) stable if, for every B ∈R, there exists C ∈R such that, for
every parameter value θ ∈Θ and every input sequence u1 · · · uT ∈U +,
∥θ∥≤B ∧
max
t∈[1..T ] ∥ut∥≤B
=⇒
max
t∈[1..T ] ∥yt∥≤C,
where yt = S(u1 · · · ut; θ) is the output at time t ∈[1..T].
G.2
Auxiliary results

Lemma 19. Every every parametric system is uniformly BIBO stable if it is uniformly BIBS stable.
Proof. Immediate by the fact that the output function of a system is a continuous function, and
continuous functions map bounded sets to bounded sets.

G.3
Stability of MinMax RNCs
Lemma 21. Every parametric MinMax Recurrent Unit is uniformly BIBS stable for every choice of
an initial state.
Proof. Let D = ⟨X, U, f; Θ | R, s⟩be a parametric MinMax Recurrent Unit, let n be the state
dimension of D, let x0 ∈X, and let B ∈R.
Let us define the following constant:
C := max

∥x0∥max, ∥R(ut; θ)∥max, ∥s(ut; θ)∥max | ut ∈U s.t. ∥ut∥≤B, θ ∈Θ s.t. ∥θ∥≤B
	
.
Note that C exists since R and s are continuous, and continuous functions map bounded sets to
bounded sets.
Let θ ∈Θ be a parameter values satisfying ∥θ∥≤B, let u1 · · · ut ∈U + be an input sequence
satisfying maxi∈[1..t] ∥ui∥≤B, and let xt = D(x0, u1 · · · ut; θ) be the state of D on input u1 · · · ut
when started from x0 and with parameters set to θ.
We first show ∥xt∥max ≤C by induction on t.
In the base case we have t = 1, and hence
x1 = D(x0, u1; θ) = f(x0, u1; θ) = (R(u1; θ) ⊗x0) ⊕s(u1; θ).
Thus, each component of x1 is a component of x0, R(u1; θ), or s(u1; θ), and hence ∥x1∥max ≤C
by construction of C.
In the inductive case we have t > 1, and we assume by induction that ∥xt−1∥max ≤C. We have
xt = D(x0, u1 · · · ut; θ) = D(xt−1, ut; θ) = f(xt−1, ut; θ) = (R(ut; θ) ⊗xt−1) ⊕s(ut; θ).
Thus, it holds that ∥xt∥max ≤C since each component of xt is a component of xt−1, R(ut; θ),
or s(ut; θ); and we have that ∥R(ut; θ)∥max, ∥s(ut; θ)∥max ≤C hold by definition of C, and that
∥xt−1∥max ≤C holds by the inductive hypothesis.
Having shown ∥xt∥max ≤C, recalling that D has state dimension n, we conclude
∥xt∥≤√n · ∥xt∥max ≤√n · C,
and hence the constant C′ := √n · C is as required.
Theorem 38. All parametric MinMax Recurrent Cascades are uniformly BIBS and BIBO stable.
Proof. By Lemma 21, every MinMax Recurrent unit is uniformly BIBS stable for every choice of
an initial state. Then, every MinMax Recurrent Layer is uniformly BIBS stable by Lemma 18, and
hence it is also uniformly BIBO stable by Lemma 19. Then, every MinMax Recurrent Cascade is
uniformly BIBS and BIBO stable by Lemma 20.
Theorem 7 (Stability). Let S be a parametric MinMax RNC having a bounded input space U and a
bounded parameter space Θ, and let Sθ be S with parameters instantiated to θ ∈Θ. There exists a
constant B ∈R such that, for every θ ∈Θ and every u1 · · · ut ∈U +, we have max{∥xt∥, ∥yt∥} ≤
B where xt and yt are the state and output of Sθ at time t on input u1 · · · ut.
Proof. It follows immediately by Theorem 38.
H
Gradient Proofs
In this appendix we prove the results from Section 6 regarding the gradient.
We first present a preliminary notions.
• Section H.1 discusses the issues with differentiating min and max functions, and it develops a
solution based on intensional derivatives for PAP functions combined with super/subgradients of
minimum norm.
• Section H.2 introduces the notion of computation graph and selected intensional derivatives com-
puted on it.
• Section H.3 defines a computation graph for the loss of MinMax RNCs, and a computation graph
for the state at time t as function of a past state.
Then in the last three sections we prove the results from Section 6.
H.1
Differentiating Min and Max Functions
For any given loss function loss (assumed to be continuously-differentiable), and any given MinMax
RNC S with input space U, output space Y , and parameter space Θ, our goal is to analyse the
gradient of the loss L(u,y)(θ) = loss(S(u; θ), y) with respect to the parameters θ on a given input
sequence u ∈U + and target output y ∈Y . The function L(u,y)(θ) is non-smooth, since it involves
min and max operations, as they occur in MinMax recurrence. In particular, the gradient of L(u,y) is
not guaranteed to exist at every point θ ∈Θ.
To provide some intuition, we discuss the case of the max function ⊕2 : R2 →R given by
⊕2(x1, x2) = x1 ⊕x2. Its gradient is well-defined at (¯x1, ¯x2) when ¯x1 ̸= ¯x2, since in this case its
change is given by the change of ¯x1 if ¯x1 > ¯x2, and by the change of ¯x2 if ¯x2 > ¯x1. However, when
¯x1 = ¯x2 the gradient of ⊕2 at (¯x1, ¯x2) does not exist.
To describe the change at all points, one can employ a subgradient of ⊕2, a generalisation of the
gradient for convex functions. In fact, the subgradient is not unique, and one refers to the set of all
subgradients as the subdifferential. One subgradient of ⊕2 at (¯x1, ¯x2) when ¯x1 = ¯x2 is given by
(1/2, 1/2), which essentially says that the change of ⊕2 is the average change of its inputs. This is in
fact the subgradient of minimum (Euclidean) norm, which is the one employed in [44].
The issue with subgradients is that they are only defined for convex functions. For concave functions
such as min, one can employ the dual notion of supergradient. However, our loss L(u,y) will not be
convex nor concave.
To address the issue, we focus on the strategy adopted in [44] to be able to always compute a
‘gradient’, with two main benefits. First, we are able to prove properties of the actual gradient,
holding almost everywhere, such as its existence and boundedness. Second, we are able to prove
properties for the ‘gradient’ computed by [44]; notably, the fact that it coincides with the gradient
almost everywhere, and that it is always bounded, even at points where the actual gradient does not
exist.
The strategy adopted in [44] is to compute a ‘gradient’ over a computation graph of L(u,y) via the
chain rule, computing locally at each node the gradient of the function for the node when such
function is differentiable, the subgradient of minimum norm in the case of a convex function, and the
supergradient of minimum norm in the case of a concave function. The result of such computation is
not guaranteed to be a gradient. Furthermore, the outcome may be different for different computation
graphs (although equivalent in the sense that they represent the same function).
However, we employ the theory of piecewise analytic under analytic partition (PAP) functions [42],
to show that L(u,y) is differentiable almost everywhere (i.e., on a subset of Θ′ ⊆Θ such that Θ \ Θ′
has measure zero), and that the ‘gradient’ computed in the above manner agrees with the gradient
almost everywhere. In particular, we show that L(u,y) is PAP; and then, for a specific choice of a
computation graph, we show that the above ‘gradient’ is an intensional derivative as defined by [42].
We introduce subgradients and supergradients, cf. [43].
Definition 34 (Subgradient). Let X = Rn, let f : X →R be a convex function, and let x ∈X. A
vector g ∈Rn is a subgradient of f at x if, for all z ∈X,
f(z) ≥f(x) + g⊤(z −x).
Then, the subdifferential ∂+
◦f(x) is the set consisting of each subgradient of f at x ∈X.
Definition 35 (Supergradient). Let X = Rn, let f : X →R be a concave function, and let x ∈X.
A vector g ∈Rn is a supergradient of f at x if −g ∈∂◦(−f)(x). Then, the superdifferential ∂+
◦f(x)
is the set consisting of each supergradient of f at x ∈X.

Proposition 7. Let f : Rn →R be the function f(x) = Ln
i=1 xi, and let I be its active variable
index function. Let {I1, . . . , Ik} = Im(I), and let A = {A1, . . . , Ak} where Ai = {x ∈Rn |
I(x) = Ii}. Then A is a partition of Rn.
Proof. First, we show that each Ai is non-empty. Since Ii ∈Im(I), there exists x ∈Rn such that
I(x) = Ii, hence x ∈Ai, and hence Ai ̸= ∅.
Second, we show Sk

I(x) = Ii.

I(x) = Ii,

and hence
Ii = Ij.
Since I1, . . . , Ik are distinct, it follows that i = j, and hence Ai = Aj.
Thus the sets of A are non-empty, pair-wise disjoint, and their union is Rn. Therefore A is a partition
of Rn.
Required background.

functions and intensional derivatives of a PAP function.

H.2
Computation Graphs and their Intensional Derivatives
Definition 38. A computation graph G of input dimension din has the following components:
• a finite set of nodes V partitioned into:
• input nodes Vin = {˚vin
1 , . . . ,˚vin
din}, arranged into a vector ˚vin = (˚vin
1 , . . . ,˚vin
din),
• computation nodes Vc;
• (multi-)edges E ⊆V ∗× Vc with each edge (˚u1, . . . ,˚un) →˚v going from a finite number of nodes
˚u1, . . . ,˚un (possibly n = 0) into a computation node ˚v; we require that each computation node
has a unique incoming edge, and that the graph is acyclic; we call n the arity of the edge, and we
call (˚u1, . . . ,˚un) the input node-vector of ˚v;
The arity of a computation node is the arity of its incoming edge. Each computation node˚v is labelled
by a function ˇv : Rn →R where n is the arity of ˚v. The output nodes Vout = {˚vout
, . . . ,˚vout
dout} ⊆V
are a subset of the nodes, arranged into a vector ˚vout = (˚vout
, . . . ,˚vout
dout), and dout is the output
dimension of the graph.
(Node vectors) A computation node-vector is a vector ˚v = (˚v1, . . . ,˚vn) of computation nodes, its
arity m is the sum of the arities of its nodes, and it is labelled by the function ˇv : Rm →Rn given by
ˇv(x1, . . . , xk) = (ˇv1(x1), . . . , ˇv1(xk)). The input node-vector of a computation node-vector ˚v is the
vector (˚u1, . . . ,˚un) where ˚ui is the input node-vector of ˚vi.
(Value functions) We define the following value functions:
• the value function of an input node˚vin
i is the function ˆvin
i : Rdin →R given by ˆvin
i (x1, . . . , xdin) =
xi;
• the value function of a computation node ˚v is the function ˆv : Rdin →R given by ˆv(x) =
ˇv(ˆu1(x), . . . , ˆun(x)) where (˚u1, . . . ,˚un) is the input node-vector of ˚v;
• the value function of a node-vector ˚v = (˚v1, . . . ,˚vk) is the function ˆv : Rdin →Rk given by
ˆv(x) := (ˆv1(x), . . . , ˆvk(x));
• the value function of the graph is the function ˆG : Rdin →Rdout given by ˆG = ˆvout;
The function represented by G is ˆG.
(Selected intensional derivative) The selected intensional derivative ˜D˚v of an input node ˚v is the
function ˜D˚v : Rdin →R given by ˜D˚v = Dˆv. The selected intensional derivative ˜D˚v of a
computation node ˚v, having input node-vector ˚u = (˚u1, . . . ,˚uk) is the function ˜D˚v : Rdin →Rdin
given by
˜D˚v(x) = ˜Dˇv
 ˆu(x)

∀x ∈Rdin.

(Restriction of the graph) Given a prefix ˚u = (˚vin
1 , . . . ,˚vin
k ) of the input vector-node of G, and a
vector u = (u1, . . . , uk) ∈Rk, the restriction G|˚u=u of G is the graph obtained by turning each
input node ˚ui into a computation node with label ˇui = ui.
■
Proposition 12. Let G be a computation graph having input dimension n, let Gu = G|˚u=u be a
restriction of G, and let m be the dimension of ˚u. Then, let ˚v be a node of G, let f be the value
function of v as a node of G, and let fu be the value function of v as a node of Gu. Then, for
every x ∈Rn−m, it holds that fu(x) = f(x, u). Consequently, for every x ∈Rn−m, it holds that
ˆG(x, u) = Gu(x).
Proof. Immediate by Definition 38.
Proposition 13. Let G be a computation graph where every label is one of the following: a
differentiable function, a max function, or a min function. Then, ˜DG is an intensional derivative of
ˆG.
Proof. Immediate by Definitions 36 and 38.
H.3
Computation Graphs of MinMax RNCs
Definition 39 (Computation graph of loss of RNC). We first introduce the context and then we give
the definitions.
Context. Let S be a MinMax RNC. Let the dimensions of S be as follows: (i) input dimension dmodel,
(ii) output dimension dmodel, (iii) model dimension dmodel, (iv) state dimension dstate, (v) units per
layer nunits, (vi) number of layers nlayers, (vii) number of parameters nparams. Let the functions of
S be as follows:
• Rℓ,k is the reset function of unit k of layer ℓ,
• sℓ,k is the set function of unit k of layer ℓ,
• hℓis the output function of layer ℓ,

Definitions. We define the following computation graphs:

Proposition 14. Let S be a MinMax RNC and let loss be as in Definition 39, and let G be the
computation graph of S for sequence length T ∈N>0 with loss function loss. Then,
(1) the value functions of G are as in Table 6;
(2) for every u ∈U T , every y ∈Y , and every θ ∈Θ, it holds that ˆG(u, y, θ) = loss(S(u; θ), y);

ˆG(u,y)(θ) = loss(S(u; θ), y)

where G(u,y) is the computation graph of S on (u, y),

Definition 40 (Computation graph of state). Let S be a MinMax Recurrent Unit, let U be its input
space, let X ⊆R be its state space, and let R, s : U →X be its input functions.
The (selected) computation graph GT of the state of S at time T ∈N>0 has:
• input node-vector ˚u = (˚u1, . . . ,˚uT ) and input node ˚x0;
• computation nodes ˚
Rt,˚st,˚zt,˚xt for each t ∈[1..T];
• output node ˚xT ;
• edges (˚
Rt,˚xt−1) →˚zt and (˚zt,˚st) →˚xt for each t ∈[1..T];
• labels as follows, for each t ∈[1..T]:
ˇRt := R,
ˇst := s,
ˇzt := min,
ˇxt := max .
The (selected) computation graph Gu of the state of S on input u ∈U + is Gu = GT |˚u=u for T the
length of the sequence u.
■
Proposition 15. Let GT be the computation graph of the state of S at time T ∈N>0 according to
Definition 40. Then, for every input sequence u ∈RT and every state x0 ∈R, we have that ˆxt(u, x0)
equals the state of S at time t on input u.
Proof. Immediate by Definition 40.
H.4
Existence and Boundedness of The Gradient of MinMax RNCs (Theorem 8)
In this section we prove Theorem 8.
Theorem 39. Let S be a MinMax RNC having input space U, output space Y , and parameter
space Θ. Let Z := U +× Y be the space of input-output pairs for S, and let loss : Y × Y →R be a
continuously-differentiable function. For each z = (u, y) ∈Z, let Gz be the computation graph of S
on z with loss function loss, and let Lz be the function Lz(θ) := loss(S(u; θ), y). Then, for every
z ∈Z, it holds that ˜DGz is an intensional derivative of Lz, there exists a subset Θz ⊆Θ such that
Θz is of full measure in Θ and
˜DGz(θ) = DLz(θ)
∀θ ∈Θz.
Furthermore, when U, Y, Θ are bounded, there exists a constant B ∈R such that, for every z ∈Z,
˜DGz(θ)
 ≤B
∀θ ∈Θ.
Proof. For every z ∈Z, by Proposition 14, we have that Gz represents Lz; namely, we have
ˆGz = Lz; hence, by Proposition 13, we have that ˜DGz is an intensional derivative of Lz; and hence,
by Proposition 8 of [42], there exists a subset Θz ⊆Θ such that Θz is of full measure in Θ and
˜DGz(θ) = DLz(θ)
∀θ ∈Θz.
In the rest of the proof we show the claimed norm bound.

Then,
∥˜DGz(θ)∥≤(nparams)1/2 · ∥˜DGz(θ)∥∞≤(nparams)1/2 · Bloss · (B1Rs · B1h)L.
As the above bound holds for every choice of z ∈Z and θ ∈Θ, we can choose
B := (nparams)1/2 · Bloss · (B1Rs · B1h)L,
and hence obtain the claimed bound
∥˜DGz(θ)∥≤B
∀z ∈Z, ∀θ ∈Θ.
This concludes the proof.
Theorem 8 (Bounded gradient). Let S be a parametric MinMax RNC with U, Y, Θ its input, output,
and parameter space, respectively. Let loss : Y × Y →R be a continuously-differentiable function,
and let Z = U +× Y be the space of input-output pairs. For each z = (u, y) ∈Z, let Lz : Θ →R
be the function Lz(θ) = loss
 S(u; θ), y

. The two following claims hold: (i) for every z ∈Z, there
is a subset Θz ⊆Θ of full measure such that the gradient ∇Lz(θ) exists for all θ ∈Θz; (ii) when U
and Θ are bounded, there is B ∈R such that ∥∇Lz(θ)∥≤B holds for all z ∈Z and all θ ∈Θz.
Proof. By Theorem 39, we have that the two following conditions hold.
(1) There exists a subset Θz ⊆Θ such that Θz has full measure in Θ and
˜DGz(θ) = DLz(θ)
∀θ ∈Θz.
(2) There exists a constant B ∈R such that
˜DGz(θ)
 ≤B
∀θ ∈Θ.
Combining the two conditions, we have
∥DLz(θ)∥≤B
∀θ ∈Θz.
As the function Lz is scalar-valued, its derivative DLz(θ) is its gradient ∇Lz(θ), and hence
∥∇Lz(θ)∥≤B
∀θ ∈Θz,
as claimed.
H.5
Non-vanishing Gradient of MinMax RNCs (Theorem 9)
In this section we prove Theorem 9.
Lemma 22. Let S be a MinMax Recurrent Unit, let U be its input space, let X = R be its state space,
and let R, s : U →X be its input functions. Furthermore, let u = (ut)t≥1 be an input sequence
where ut ∈U for all t ≥1, and let xu
t : X →X be the function such that xu
t (x0) is the state of S at
time t on input (u1, . . . , ut) starting from state x0 ∈X. It holds that
Dxu
t (x0) = 1
for all x0 ∈(at, bt),
where at = max{s(u1), . . . , s(ut)} and bt = min{R(u1), . . . , R(ut)}.
Proof. For each T ∈N, let us define
γT := {⟨AT , id⟩},
where AT is the open interval AT := (aT , bT ). We show that γT is a PAP representation of
xu
T |(aT ,bT ). First, we have that {(aT , bT )} is trivially an analytic partition of (aT , bT ), since (aT , bT )
is analytic noting that (aT , bT ) ⊆R is an open domain as required by Definition 3 of [42].
Then, we show by induction on T that
xu
T |(aT ,bT ) = id.
(105)
Namely, we show
xu
T (x0) = x0
∀x0 ∈(aT , bT ).
(106)
In the base case T = 0, and we have xu
0(x0) = x0 by definition for all x0 ∈R. In the inductive case
we have T > 0, and assuming xu
T −1|(aT −1,bT −1) = id by induction, we have, for all x0 ∈(aT , bT ),
xu
T (x0) =
 R(uT ) ⊙xu
T −1(x0)

⊕s(uT )
| def. of xu
T
=
 R(uT ) ⊙x0

⊕s(uT )
| inductive hypothesis since x0 ∈(aT , bT ) ⊆(aT −1, bT −1)
= x0 ⊕s(uT )
| x0 < bT ≤R(uT )
= x0,
| x0 > aT ≥s(uT )
as required.
Thus, by Lemma 15 of [42], we have Dxu
T |(aT ,bT ) = DγT since (aT , bT ) is the interior of γT ; then,
by (105), we have DγT = Did; finally, Did = 1. Therefore,
Dxu
T (x0) = DγT (x0) = Did(x0) = 1
∀x0 ∈(aT , bT ),
as claimed.
Theorem 9 (Non-vanishing gradient). Let S be a MinMax Neuron with input space U, state space
X = R, and input functions (R, s). Let u = u1 · · · ut ∈U +, and let xu
t : X →X be the function
such that xu
t (x0) is the state of S at time t on input u starting from x0 ∈X. We have ∇xu
t (x0) = 1
for all x0 ∈(at, bt), where at = max{s(u1), . . . , s(ut)} and bt = min{R(u1), . . . , R(ut)}.
Proof. Immediate by Lemma 22.
We show next an additional result: not only the state gradient is one for the range specified above, but
also its selected intensional derivative is one.
